is most comforting in allaying tickling and irrita-
tion in the throat, and giving strength to the voice,
and it never allows a cough or asthma to become
chronic. No house should be without it, as, taken
at the beginning, a dose is generally sufficient,
and a complete cure is certain.

Various Verses,
AMONG THE PALMS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN].
To save the past by one brave deed 1 (|
The time was “now,” the place “this spot.
Love-stifled rage importunes speed,
For Hate can thrive where Love will rot.
A noiseless step, a whispered word,
A man clasped in a woman’s arms 1
The white-faced watcher saw and heard,
And beckoned Death—among the palms.
He kissed a dagger’s silvered hilt —
Her gift once in the long ago ;
Then whisp’ring Heaven —“ Forgive her guilt!
He freed her with a single blow.
And who shall say ’twas ill or good
Who reads not the Recorder’s scroll.
An angel came and caught his blood ;
A devil laughed and took his soul.
I only see what might have been —
A girl locked in a dead man’s arms;
A traitor slain, a stain wiped clean—
Among the palms, among the palms.
N.S.W.
HESKETH.
OUR COMMENTATOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In a dingy little chamber, where the massive four-
post bed _ 'I
Seems annoyed because the ceiling presses closely
overhead,
Where the meat-safe crowds the washstand, and
the table crowds the chairs,
We engage in daily struggles with our literary
cares.
We are two aspiring authors who are striving all
we know
To earn enough to keep us till the talents that we
show
Shall gain us recognition— then we’ll take an extra
room
That shall mark our own advancement and “ Our
Commentator’s ” doom !
For we have a Commentator, one who chatters all
the time
When we need our whole attention for an awk-
ward bit of rhyme,
Keeps suggesting hopeless subjects, or inanely
volunteers
Some ridiculous perversion of our loftiest ideas ;
Who, though innocent of grammar and the rudi-
ments of verse,
Offers criticisms freely, which are usually adverse;
And insists on our explaining all discussions that
are rife;
And we have to grin and bear it—she’s the younger
author’s wife !
And she fancies she is helping and she shows a
marked delight
When an article’s accepted, or we get a tale to
write; , !
And when funds are at their lowest and there s
nothing for the pot,
She will pawn her Sunday petticoat as cheerfully
as not.
But, if she shares our troubles, she demands an
equal share I
Of anything that’s going when we’ve any cash to
spare; j
She claims a fair division of our beer and cigar-
ettes ; I
And ’tis not the smallest portion she invariably
gets !
And at times she’s very mournful and at others
very gay,
And which is most disturbing it is difficult to say,
For she’s generally saddest when we strike a
merry vein,
And particularly lively when we seek a solemn
strain !
When we’ve work that must be finished and we
have to sit up late,
The man who didn’t marry her must go outside
and wait
Until the lady goes to bed—but not, alas !to rest, ,
For it’s then “ Our Commentator ” is distinctly at
her best!
From behind the close-drawn curtains of the big, j
old-fashioned bed
She hurls her interruptions and her comments at
our head ;
If we won’t take any notice, then her face will
soon appear,
And she’ll give us her opinion of our efforts with
a sneer. j
But we worry through it, somehow, till at last the
work is done,
Which is very, very often at the rising of the sun ;
And, as the lucky husband shows his partner to ,
the door,
“ Our Commentator ” speeds him with a valedic-
t’ry snore! j
And, if our contributions fail to win us lasting
fame,
It may be consolation that we’ve someone we
can blame ; i >
And, if we prove successful, when our reputation’s
made
We will willingly pay tribute to Our Commen-
tator’s ” aid.
And if our future efforts host le critics choose to
slash, .
We’ve had sufficient training not to wince beneath
the lash ;
And successful work can’t render us inordinately j
vain
Whilst “Our Commentator” shows it such in- |
effable disdain.
Melbourne.
G. G.

“ Yanhee Doodle, Mended with Politics,
The above is, of course, a caricature of the famous American national picture, which will be
given later on.
“YANKEE DOODLE” is everywhere spoken of as the finest plug-tobacco that has ever
appeared here, and it has won greater success during the short time it has been on the market than
anything previously. This is no idle boast, and, to prove it, any smoker can teat the statement, for
there are few towns in N.S.W. where “YANKEE DOODLE” is not known and sold. Its sale
has this year increased nearly one hundred (100) per cent over that of last year. And why is this?
Simply that “YANKEE DOODLE” has struck the popular taste. In the first place, it comes, after
years of careful study, from a combination of certain choice Virginian tobaccos, so blended and
exquisitely flavoured, that any smoker who can stand a tobacco heavier than the pure gold-leaf is
forced to admit that he has at last discovered something far and away better than anything he has
been accustomed to. DIXSON & SONS have taken over half-a-century to accomplish this; and, as
their leading business has been built up on their reputation for QUALITY, they could not afford tc
make the foregoing statement if any doubt existed. DIXSON & SONS, who use more Aim fican leaf
than all the other Tobacco Factories combined, are noted for several high-class brands“ CON-
QUEROR,” “SIR ROGER,” “CHAMPION,” and “DOLLAR;” but “YANKEE DOODLE’
ranks supreme.

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 2, 1895.
Premier Reid and Federation.
The almost-forgotten project of Australian
Federation was disinterred from its ashes
last week, when the Federal Enabling Bill
was quietly introduced in N.S.W. Assembly.
It is four years since the sitting of the first
Federal Conference and nearly one year
since the second one, and for most of that
time the federal project has peacefully slum-
bered. At each conference N.S. Wales led
the way, and after each conference that pro-
vince fell asleep so conspicuously that at
last the adjacent States decided to move no
further in the matter until something definite
was done by the Parliament at Sydney.
When that unexpected event happened the
Governments of Victoria, Tasmania, and
South Australia pledged themselves to fol-
low suit, so that the federal movement has
for a long while back been waiting until the
N.S.W. Government should see fit to take
some further interest in the matter. But
most politicians either regarded the subject
as dead, or had forgotten that it ever was
alive, and the appearance the other day of
the long-delayed measure was a considerable
surprise. Its reception was a sad and solemn
proceeding. Premier Reid certainly intro-
duced the scheme with the air of a man who
was in dead earnest, but thereafter a number
of members rose and stated in listless tones
that Federation was£a question in which
they took a great and living interest, and
added, with a weary aspect, that they had
always been enthusiastic in the matter.
Then the subject died standing, in a manner
of speaking, and the Bill which is intended
to make Austral a a nation was brought in
amid so little interest that it might almost
as well have been a Saturday night drunk
being carried into the lock-up on a wheel-
barrow.
• • •
Even at this early stage of the proceed-
ings, however, there are signs that the
curse of party politics is going to interfere.
Ex - Treasurer See asked contemptuously
whether tlie Federal Bill was worth intro-
ducing, and Lyne and CoPt.land demanded
that the House should abandon the workable
scheme agreed upon at the last conference’
and go back to the hopelessly unworkable
one devised by the Convention of ’9l. The
scheme of the ’9l conference was abandoned
not only by New South Wales but also by
all the other provinces, so that if one pro-
vince reverted to it the others would almost
certainly refuse to follow its example. Also,
the men who made it are almost all politi-
cally dead and gone, and some are personally
deceased as well. Even their names—
Pakkes, Dibbs, Jennings, Grey, Atkinson,
Griffith, MHlwraith, Macrossan, Play-
ford, Bray, Munro, Gillies, Shibls, and a
lot more—read like ancient history ; there
has been such a great and almost unani-
mous downfall among these constitution-
makers of ’9l that Australia has nearly
forgotten them along with their clumsy,
ill-devised project. The proposal that New
South Wales should go back all alone to
the forgotten ideas of four years ago, and
thereby compel the other provinces to do
likewise or else bring about another dead-
lock, is a fine sample of the brutal stupidity
of party politics. The Lyne party, it is
true, has some semblance of a grievance in
the matter. But for the ceaseless obstruc-
tion of the Opposition it might, or, again, it
might not, under the Dibbs Government of
1891-’94, have actually passed the original
Federal Bill, and then Lyne, Dibbs, Cope-
land and their friends might have gone down
to history among the Fathers of the Common-
wealth. But the country has not time to
consider the personal grievances of small
politicians, and the Government which lost
its opportunity, whether by its own fault or
not, must do without it. Nobody wants to
know now about what might have been but
wasn't, and no one cares about the merits,
whatever they might be, of the original Con-
vention scheme, because that scheme has
died and become impracticable through old
age. The one thing to be considered is that
there is a chance of carrying out a new
scheme now, provided the New South Wales
Legislature, wliich has so long blocked the
way, does not lose time mooning over the
dry bones of the past, and scattering recrimi-
nations about the personal grievances of its
members.
The present Federal scheme, of which
Premier Reid is one of the authors, is in-
comparably superior to the one which the
Dibbs Government failed to enact a year or
two ago. Under the original project a
certain Bill was to be submitted to the
various Parliaments of Australasia, and
when they all passed exactly the same
Bill, the Commonwealth would be estab-
lished. But it would be absolutely impos-
sible to prevent the various Parliaments
making alterations in the original measure,
and it would be equally impossible to induce
them all to make exactly the same altera-
tions, and the prospect of ever arriving at
entire unanimity was very remote indeed.
The device of the more recent Hobart con-
ference gets over this d.fiiculty. Each pro-
vince is to elect ten repres ntatives, and
these are to form a convention and draft a
constitution for the Commonwealth. If
New South Wales and only two other pro-
vinces adopt the constitution thus drafted
ttie Commonwealth will make a start as soon
afterwards as possible, the other provinces
being left to come in later on, as the dis-
advantages of isolation become more and
more apparent to them. There is no pro-
vision for amending the constitution before
the Commonwealth is started,’for to allow
amendments by the separate Parliaments
would simply be to bring about tire deadlock
wliich inevitably awaited the scheme of ’9l.
.The constitution must be wholly rejected, or
else accepted as it stands, however that may
be, and the Federal Legislature must amend
it afterwards wherever repair is necessary.
In short, it is recognised that the first
essential is to get the best kind of Federation
that circumstances admit of to begin with,
and make it better as things go on, instead
of waiting in the foolish hope of getting an
ideal Federation to begin with.
* « *
The arrangements provide that all the
provinces shall be equally represented on
the convention which is to prepare the plans
and specifications of the Commonwealth.
Therefore, Westralia and Tasmania will have
as commanding a voice as New South Wales
or Victoria, though either of these has five
times the population of the first two com-
bined. Mr. Lyne carped at this as one of
the reasons why the scheme of the Hobart
convention should be abandoned, but it is,
in The Bulletin’s opinion, a wise and
statesmanlike proviso. It was necessary, or
at least advisable, to make the concession to
induce the smaller provinces to join in the
movement, but it is quite probable that the
convention, created on these lines, will build
as good a constitution as would have been
produced by one elected on a population
basis. If it doesn’t, thy superior voting
power of New South Wales and Victoria in
the Federal Parliament will enable them to
repair things later on according to their own
wisdom, or want of wisdom, as the case may
be What the constitution thus prepared
will be like no one knows ; the country of
necessity “goes it blind” in the matter.
But providing that it is based iu a
general sense on popular suffrage, and that
it does not create any legislative body
so constituted as to be absolutely indepen-
dent of the popular will, and therefore im-
pervious to amendment, it should be adopted
whatever its other defects. If the propos id
Federal Legislature is amenable, either
directly or indirectly, to the popular vote,
then the new Australian nation can pre-
sently make it as democratic as it pleases
and as perfect as it knows how —in other
words, it can make it as good a Legislature
as the country deserves to have. Every
country which is ruled by the people gets
in the end just as good a system of govern-
ment as the people deserve.
* * *
There was a time, a few years ago, when
The Bulletin troubled itself considerably
over the details of the Commonwealth. In
those days there was some semblance of
popular interest in the subject, and it seemed
possible that the Parliaments might take the
matter up with something almost resembling
enthusiasm. Since then, however, this paper
has gradually come to realise the length and
breadth and depth and general vastness of
the public indifference, and the unlikelihood
of getting either the people or the Legisla-
tures to devote any prolonged attention to
the matter. Everybody approves of Aus-
tralian unity in the abstract, but hardly any-
body approves of it with much interest or
energy. Therefore, The Bulletin of to-day
will be glad to see the arrival of almost any
kind of a Federation, provided only that its
badness is not so elaborately devised as to be
wholly incurable. When it arrives the com-
munity will take an interest in it as a matter
of necessity, and will amend it so far as the
sense ot the community goes, and in course
of time Australia, as already mentioned, will
get as good a system of government as it is
worthy of. Then there will be an end of the
dreary wrangling about petty local tariffs,
and of the dispute about the debateable land
on the S.A. border, and the expense and
waste of the border customs will be done
away with, and the war of railway rates will
be ended, and also the building of unprofit-
able lines along the various frontiers with a
view to stealing the trade of the adjacent
province, and instead of six separate grovels
to as many distinct Governors, there will, let
us hope, be only one grovel at the most.
Also, there will be a chance of constructing
a really good central Government such as
this country has never yet possessed. Great
Britain, with nearly forty million people to
choose from, often finds it difficult to supply
material for one thoroughly capable Minis-
try, and Australasia, with only four millions,
has fully demonstrated that it can’t furnish
seven honest and brainy Cabinets, and seven
intelligent Parliaments all at once. Butitpro-
bably can, out of these four millions, supply
at least one Government which will be suffi-
ciently capable to bear reasonable inspection,
and if it fails to do so it will be mainly the
nation’s own fault. Also, it will have an
opportunity to consolidate the public debt
at a greatly reduced rate of interest,
and to settle t! i Chinese question
for all Australia, and to shut down on the
Queensland Kanaka and the coolie and other
forms of cheap labor, and to prohibit all
future land-grant railway jobbery, and
if it fails to do these things also it will
again be the fault of the Australian people
as a whole. The establishment of almost
any kind of a central Government will give
Australians in general a voice in all such
matters, and if they are not settled satis-
factorily it can only be because the majority
of the people don’t deserve - through their
ignorance or indifference or stupidity—that
they should be. /
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Fourteen Years.
The sentence of 14 years’ penal servitude
passed on George Dean, for perjury and
making a false declaration, is a remarkable
propf of the disadvantages of being a celebrated
criminaL Dean’s nominal offence was that he
swore falsely to save his neck from the hang-
man, but it would seem that the worst part cf
his crime was that he fooled the people of N.S.
Wales, and made the Government look more or
less ridiculous, and was the indirect means of
bringing down obloquy on a prominent Judge
and on the Attorney-General and sundry
members of the bar, and on the Crown Law
Department, and that he gained sympathy
under false pretences, and thereby made a
large and influential section of the public
feel, when it looked back at itself, that
it had been a very great ass indeed. Dean
induced the great, illogical people to cheer for
him, and drink his health, and come long
distances to shake hands with him, and
dedicate waltz-music to him, and hug
him publicly, and, in short, he had a
loan of the community in many different
ways. And the natural end of such a pro-
ceeding is that he gets the heaviest sentence
which the law will allow. A few years ago a
girl, also in Sydney, swore falsely that one
Buttner had committed a capital offence on
her, and by hard, continuous, and wholly
gratuitous lying, she got him sentenced to death.
She did it, apparently, from mere vice, cruelly,
and bloodthirstiness. She had not, like Dean,
the excuse that she was driven to perjury by
the instinct of self-preservation ; her only
apparent motive was the desire to hang some-
body by way of amusement and sensationalism.
She was proved by good evidence and on her
own confession to have been a perjurer and an
intended murderer and a person who en-
deavoured to pervert justice ; in short, she was
exactly what Dean was in every detail. But
she didn’t get 14 years in gaol; she wasn’t
even fined 2s. 6d. and costs; in fact, she
wasn’t even arrested, nor was any at-
tempt made to arrest her. Since her
time, half-a-dozen other vicious females have
comurtted the same perjury and attempted
mu u ml perversion of justice in different
pats of New South Wales, and, despite the
clearest proof of their guilt, not one of them
has been arrested for it. The Bulletin has a
vague recollection that one of the tribe, who
attempted by gratuitous perjury and through
pure cussedness to send a complete stranger to
lifelong imprisonment or the gallows, once got
three months in Victoria, but, otherwise,
criminals of this class have been allowed,
and are still allowed, to flourish absolutely un-
checked. Considering that the law abso-
lutely refuses even to know of their exis-
tence, and comparing its somnolence in
their cases with its enthusiasm and vigor in the
much less atrocious case of Dean, it is evident
that there is a serious discrepancy somewhere.
The Exemption Muddle.
\
The present situation as regards the N.S.W.
Land-tax Bill is about as complex as anything
well can be. In the first place, the President
of the Council ruled that the Upper House
couldn’t lawfully amend the Bill. The Speaker
of the Assembly afterwards ruled that it could
make amendments, and the Council accepted
his decision—partly because the Speaker is the
highest local authority on cbhstiluiidnal points,
and partly because his ruling suited,it ‘’down to
the ground.” Accordingly it amended the Bill
by striking out the <£475 exemption altogether.
Premier Reid now declares that the Council’s
action is unconstitutional, but in the face of
Speaker Abbott’s pronouncement to the con-
trary, his statement amounts to very little or
nothing. At last election the province affirmed,
with tolerable distinctness, its adhesion to the
land-tax principle, but that is about all it diii!
It couldn’t state definitely, by the cumbersome
and indefinite process of a general election,
whether it wanted a land-tax with exemptions,
or with this particular exemption, or without
any exemption at all. But it may be assumed
that the people who have no land and no money
invested on mortgage mostly approve of the
tax, because everybody approves of taxing
the other man. Also, the people who
come under the exemption mostly approve
for the same reason ; wdiile the people
who are not exempt disapprove strongly.
These latter either want no land-tax at all, or
else they want one with no exemptions what-
ever, so that they may have the profound joy
of seeing the small owner—who voted for the
tax under the impression that it w ould only hit
the other man—hoisted with his own petard.
As most of the voting population owns no land
there will probably still be a majority in favor
of the tax, even without the exemptions. But
it w’ill be mainly among th urban population,
and in ia good many agrimltural electorates
wheFfe the small holders v Red for the land-tax
policy under the imprtssi m that they would go
free, there is likely to be a serious change of
sentiment and a general tendency to c use the
Reid Government, if the Council’s policy pre-
vails. The Reid Ministry is pledged to die if
necessary in defence of the exemptions, and
it will have to de r end them or alienate
many of its supporters. The Council will pro-
bably stand to its position ; there is a holy joy
in taxing the small owners who voted so enthu-
siastically for taxing the large owners, and the
rich landlords of the Upper House appreciate
the situation. The Government’s organ, the
Daily Telegraph, blows hot and cold; it
declares a land impost without exemptions to
be an ideal tax, but it professes to favor the
exemptions rather than . lose the Bill alto-
gether. Therefore, if it can persuade the
Ministry and the Assembly to accept the
Council’s ideas on the subject it will
be all the better satisfied. Probably, for the
sake of the extra £400,000 of revenue, Premier
Reid would also be better satisfied, but (o
knuckle down to the Council in this matter
would mean ruin sooner or later. Meanwhile,
The Bulletin desires to point out for the
fiftieth time that the Land-tax should be a
measure for the encouragement of small hold-
ings and to prevent the accumulation of big
estates, and the establishment of a squirearchy
and a rack-rented tenantry and a servile peasant
class. The exemption-clause would tend this
way, by making small holdings more profitable
than large ones, and if it is sacrificed there is
not very much left in the Land-tax portion of
the Bill that is worth preserving.
One Law for the Jew.
“ The Abrahams are being prosecuted not
because they are ofl'enders against the law, but
because they are Jews,” said Barrister Duffy,
a month or two back, in Melbourne Supreme
Court, when the quartette of gun-forgers were
being tried, and the assertion drew down a
severe rebuke from Bench, Parliament, and
press. Yet in the light of later events Mr.
Duffy’s assertion would appear to have been
based on an extremely solid substratum of
fact. Since the Abrahams were sent to
gaol four persons concerned in another Hebrew
firm were found guilty of defrauding the Cus-
toms Department, and were also awarded terms
of imprisonment varying from three months to
one year. Since this .conviction no less than
four allegedly Christian business concerns have
been discovered in the act of defrauding the
Government. In each of these cases, the Cus-
toms Department was satisfied to accept a big
fine ; and there was no hint of criminal pro-
ceedings. But the latest cases are even
more glaring. For some time past, the Customs
Department has had its eye upon sundry wine-
anil spirit firms of go lly repute and unquestion-
able antecedents, and, after the collection of
sundry evidence, it became possessed of the fact
that these same highly-respectable mercantile
houses were selling locally-made spirits as the
products of noted French and other distilleries.
The cases, in fact, seemed to the lay mind
to amount to obtaining money under false-
pretences, yet a civil prosecution in a minor
court was the only course taken. There
one merchant was fined £lO, with £7 7s. costs,
for causing a label of “ Laurier et Cie., vintage
1585,” to be placed on brandy which had never
seen Laurier’s. Another firm, which bail in
its possession a quantity of Warrenheip
brrndy, also labelled as Laurier’s, was fined
£lO, with £l2 12s. costs. The prosecution
of another firm was for some reason
stopped, and a fourth firm was mulct in £lO,
with £lO 10s. costs. At the conclusion of the


cases the president of the Beach warned the
malefactors that a repetition of the offence
would mean gaol without imprisonment. Mean-
while the four Abrahamses, Rosenjfeld, Ben-
jamin, and Davies are whiling away the long
summer afternoons with a two-pound hammer
and a heap of bluestoue spalls, or something
like that. Again, the Chosen race appear to be
chosen with a vengeance.
The Crentle Kanaka.
The virtues of the docile and “reliable”
Kanaka, writes a Bulletin correspondent, are
being brought forcibly home to the persons, if
not to the intellects, of the people of the Bunda-
berg (Q.) district. Every Saturday night for
the past month the dusky savages have held
a tribal fight in the streets of the town, and the
utmost vigilance of the police has been unable
to prevent bloody encounters. A few Satur-
days ago the police watched the different points
of ingress to the town and disarmed the mur-
derous wretches as they arrived. Four loaded
six-chambered revolvers, hundreds of bludgeons,
leaden missiles, axes and tomahawks re-
warded their search. But the climax
came last Saturday night, when a fierce
tribal row occurred in the main street between
the Gala and Malayta “boys,” who “went for”
one another with much fury. The com-
batants turned on the police when they
intervened, and some of the constables
were injured. The savages, were, how-
ever, dispersed, and the townspeople again
breathed freely. But on Sunday morning
(20th Sept.) they were rudely awakened from
the delights of their fool’s paradise, by the
announcement that an inoffensive white had
been brutally murdered by Kanakas near
Rubyanna plantation, about five miles from
town. Joseph Lawtv, a selector, had been
done to death on a sequestered road near the
plantation, and his body was found there on
the Sunday morning. The man (about 35 years
old) had left his house to seek an errant child,
when he met his death. The body was literally
hacked to pieces by the savage brutes. He was
apparently encountered by a gang of Kanakas
armed with tomahawks and knives, and slain
within 150 yards of a neighbor’s house and
about twice that distance from his own domi-
cile. His collar-bone was cut through by a
blow of a tomahawk, the cut extending down
to the lungs. A portion of his scalp was
cut away by another blow, while his spine
was cut through in several places. Each
of the murderers apparently inflicted a wound
on the prostrate body on his own behalf, as is
the custom amongst the Polynesians in crimes
of this sort. Some seven natives of Malayta
have since been arrested charged with the
murder, and it is understood that one at least
has turned “Queen’s evidence.” The effect of
this hideous crime has been a universal cry
from the people, “ the Kanaka must go.” The
district is now in a state of white terror, and
in the country parts few have the temerity to
venture out after nightfall. This is the second
murder by Kanakas here within the year. As
the close of the crushing season is rapidly
approaching, the public realise that hundreds
of these murderous blacks will be thrown out
of employment by the genial planters and
allowed to “walk about” until next season.
The consequences likely to attend this can
easily be conceived. With bands of the planters’
pets roaming about the town and country, sans
work, saws food, sans moral responsibility, deeds
of violence can be counted upon. The boys arrested
have all been long-time residents of the pro-
vince, and came for years under the civilising (?)
influence of the Christian missionaries. The
tree of missionary zeal is badly recom-
mended by its fruit. The next Buuda-
berg election will be settled in one act
by this crime: no friend of the slave-trade
will be elected again. The planters look on ap-
parently unconcerned, and seem to feel no ill
effects of black-labor so long as their own hides
escape untouched. Independent of these
crimes, daily wrong is being done to the white
men by the presence of the niggers. White
laborers’ wages in the sugar industry have gone
down to zero (most people will agree that 7s. fid.
a week is zero) and even then the Kanakas are
employed at white men’s work in and out of
the mills, in contravention of the law and
the regulations. There might about as
well be no law at all prohibiting the
employment of the Kanakas in the mills, for
the planters completely ignore it, and the
supervision by the Polynesian Department is
such a screaming farce as to make even the
Kanakas themselves laugh at it. The dis-
district is virtually given over to the planters
and their slave-labor, while hundreds of white
men, able and eager for work, are humping
bluey all round looking in vain for employment.
Law Reform.
The law-reform scheme of Premier Kingston
(S.A.) sits right down on some of the worst
abuses of the legal ring. It practically throws
open the profession of the law to the world at
large by providing that any person, male or
female, who can pass a searching examination
in local law, and who has bad two years experi-
ence as an articled law-clerk, shall be eligible
for admission to the profession. All the stipu-
lations about going through a University course,
attending lectures, grinding up useless dead
languages, and so on, which have been intro-
duced so as.to block admission into the legal
ring, are to be done away with in one act. The
comparatively simple and inexpensive proce-
dure of the local courts is to apply to the
Supreme Court also, and many cases which
have hitherto been tried in the Supreme,Court
are hereafter to be dealt with by the inferior
tribunals. In certain cases solicitors and
barristers are to be wholly excluded. Pro-
vision is made for the settlement of appeals for
the small fee of £5 in the Supreme Court and
£2 in the local Court; no lawyers being allowed
to appear on either side. No solicitor can
recover costs from a client until his bill has
been taxed. Barristers are authorised to sue
the solicitors employing them for their fees.
Also—and this is the worst knock of all—a
barrister who takes up a case and doesn’t attend
to it, after the good old barristerial style,
is made liable to be sued for damages
arising out of his negligence. Hitherto it has
been the barrister’s privilege to take a big
fee, and then turn up in court or not, just as he
pleased, and if he didn’t turn up, or if he
arrived but proved to have not even read his
brief, and to be in a state of blank ignorance
about the case, the client had to stand the con-
sequences and feel as resigned as he could.
Therefore, in laying a sacrilegious hand on this
privilege Kingston blasphemes the sacred Ark
of the Covenant itself, and the magnates of the
horsehair-profession are thirsting for his blood
to the last shark.
Another Privileged Shark.
The truly blessed privilege of being a lawyer is
again exemplified in the case of Thomas Ernest
Rofe, a Gent. One, of Sydney. Rofe was the
plaintiff’s solicitor in the infamous Butler
divorce-suit, and he conducted a case which
was from first to last a glaring conspiracy. The
law finally decided that Rofe’s client was a
perjurer and a hirer of perjured witnesses, and
it gave him and his witnesses up to seven years
in gaol apiece. The same law decided that
Rofe had conspired with his perjured client
and his client’s perjured witnesses, and that he
was deeply involved in the attempt to pervert
justice ; and it sentenced him to five years’ gaol.
But, with the express sanction and approval of
the New South Wales Crown-law authorities, he
was released under the First Offenders Act, and
so he goes cheerfully back to his luxurious
home, a free man, and will not serve a single
hour of his purely nominal sentence. The in-
ference drawn by the public will be that a law-
yer can commit almost any crime he pleases
without having to chew hominy and crack
stones. Rofe is the only man in the whole
batch of convicts who gets off scot-free.
Butler and his witnesses are left to
work out their respective sentences. Johnson,
who was convicted of perjuring himself on
behalf of the defence, not for money or any
other sordid motive, but in the manly (if mis-
guided) effort to save his innocent and brutally-
used sister from social ruin, wasn’t released
under the First Offenders’ Act, or any other
Act. Johnson’s case, at the worst, practically
was that he saw his sister overwhelmed by the
perjured evidence brought against her by Rofe
and his fellow conspirators, and he proceeded
to swear rather recklessly on her side to pull
her through, and, as a consequence, the Law has
decided that he must serve out his three-year
sentence. But Rofe, who was convicted of
cold-bloodedly conspiring for a mean, sordid
fee to blast the good name of an
innocent woman, and whose action was
an indirect but powerful incentive to Johnson’s
crime, is treated on a wholly different plan.
There are many redeeming features about John-
son’s case, but about the offence of the heart-
less, soulless attorney there is not, so far as the
evidence goes, even one. His release will still
further convince the public that the ordinary
processes of law don’t apply to the well-con-
nected attorney, and the fact that his release
was stated to have been specifically asked for
or assented to or smiled upon by the Crown Law
office sounds like a wholly unjustifiable interfer-
ence by one lot of lawyers on behalf of another.
The law, by registering a sentence of three
years against Johnson and of five years
against Rofe, expressed a distinct opinion
that the latter was much the worse criminal of
the two. And now the public wants to know
if the worse criminal was let off because he was
a lawyer, and the less offender was gaoled
because he wasn’t one ? Lawyer Heydon’s
remarks about Rope’s mental sufferings and
the “ respectable position ”of his brothers and
sisters furnish no explanation whatever. John-
son also had mental sufferings, and if his sister
didn’t occupy a “ respectable position ” at the
time of his sentence, that was largely Rofe’s
fault, and the law has no more right to weep
about one man’s sister than about the other
man’s. Have the N.S.W. Parliament and
public sunk so low that this kind of thing is to
be endured ?
Insulted by Act of Parliament.
One of the clauses in the New South Wales
Civil Service Bill provides that no relation by
blood or marriage of any one of the Civil
Service Commissioners shall be appointed to
the service while the Commissioner he is related
to holds office. This opens up a large question.
A considerable portion of the press opposes the
proviso on the ground that it is the business of
the State to find men who are honest enough to
be trusted with an important office without
this “ degrading restriction,” and denounces it
as a “deliberate insult.” This is a specious
argument which looks as if it had something in
it. Nepotism is one of the most common forms
of corruption in the public service, and the
Public Service Bill makes a special effort to
prevent it. By doing so it distinctly implies
that the best kind of Commissioner it expects
to get can’t be wholly trusted, and by putting
him under this special restriction it casts
an insulting reflection on his honesty. At
the same time the world is so full of insulting
reflections, that, in The Bulletin’s opinion, one
more doesn’t matter. Everybody in the public
service who is entrusted with cash is insulted
by having his accounts audited, which distinctly
implies that the Government is much afraid
lest he has been peculating the funds. He is
also required to produce vouchers, which means
that the Government suspects him of lying and
cooking the accounts. The work of every
public official is supposed to be overhauled by
somebody, because he can’t be trusted without
being overhauled. Even the Colonial Treasurer’s
accounts are examined to see if he has been
taking any of the money ; and Judges’ decisions
are frequently reviewed by the Full Court, which
implies a doubt about their law and common
sense, or else about their impartiality. Every-
body, in short, in the State service is insulted
by somebody else, the insult being carefully
adapted to the circumstances of each case.
Even the appointment of the Commissioners is
a direct insult to Parliament, as it deliberately
expresses an opinion that Parliament isn’t
honest enough or intelligent enough to run the
service any longer. Under these circumstances,
it is only in keeping with the general sym-
metry of things that the Commissioners should
be insulted in the same fashion as other people.
All the auditing and inspecting and bye-laws
and regulations are based on the theory that
the public servant, whether he be a permanent
under-secretary or a laud-agentat Mud Flat, will
do something very wrong at the earliest oppor-
tunity unless he is kept under strict supervision.
The land-agent’s accounts are examined because
embezzlement would be the most convenient
and probable sin in his case. The C.S. Com-
missioner is forbidden, to give billets to his rela-
tives because nepotism is the handiest form of
corruption in his particular instance, and if the
one precaution is an insult so is the other.
The Victorian Factory Bill.
The Factories and Shops Bill, introduced the
other day into the Victorian Parliament by
Chief Secretary Peacock is about the
straightest attempt to tackle the sweating evil
yet made in Australia. For a long time the
community found it difficult to take Peacock
seriously by reason of his youthfulness and his
dreadful laugh, nevertheless he is gradually
coming to be recognised as a brainy Democrat
and a man with great possibilities in front of
him. Whether his Bill proves a success or a
failure, its existence testifies that Peacock and
his colleagues are men with pluck enough to
tackle a dreadfully complex question, which
most politicians have hitherto shirked by reason
of its vastness and apparent hopelessness. The
Vic. Government commences at the worst end
of the evil—the sinful sweating of the sewing
woman who works in her own home out
of the public sight and beyond the ken of the
factory inspector. In most cases the sewing
woman would as soon work in her employer’s
factory or workshop, but there she would come
more or less under public observation, and
factory inspectors and trades union authorities
would take note of her gauntness and misery,
and her unspeakable rate of pay, and conse-
quently her employer doesn’t want her
there on any account. Peacock’s Bill pro-
vides, in the first place, that no work
is to be “ given out,” unless the person
who gets it has a permit, said permit to be
granted only if she is an invalid, or is detained
at home by domestic responsibilities. The prices
for all kinds of sewing-work done by women
and children are to be fixed by a board con-
sisting of two representatives of the employers,
two representatives of the employes, and an
arbitrator mutually agreed upon. Women and
children are almost w’holly unprotected by
trades-unionism, therefore they constitute by
far the worst-sweated section of the com-
munity, and as there is no prospect that they
will ever be able to unite for their own effectual
protection, the Turner Ministry rightly pro-
poses to interfere on their behalf. The new
system has the great advantage of publicity ;
three-fourths of the villainous things that are
done under the sweating system, often by high-
placed snufflebusters and pillars of the church,
would never have begun to take place if they
had to happen in the light of day. It is also
proposed to limit the hours of shop-assistants —
another class which works unspeakable hours
and seems incapable of effective combination ;
to limit the number of apprentices, it having
been found that the apprentice system is abused
simply in order to get a man’s or a woman’s
work done for a small child’s pay ; and to also
limit the number of alleged “improvers,” who
represent the apprentice evil in another and
often a worse form. No Chinese factory or
workshop-owner is to be permitted to sleep in
his workroom, or to work on Sunday, or later
than 5 p.m. from Monday to Friday, and
2 p.m. on Saturdays,— a strictly logical
proviso based on the broad ground that the
Chow has no more right to sweat his fellow
Chow than the white man has to sweat his
Caucasian Laundries, dye-works,
&c., are to be included among factories and
made open to inspection, and inspectors are in
future to have power to summarily close in-
sanitary factories and workrooms. Altogether
the measure seems calculated to do the maxi-
mum of good with the minimum of fuss and
interference, and if Chief Secretary Peacock
succeeds in engineering it through Parliament
his horrid laugh should be remembered with
gratitude through the years of Victoria’s
future history.
Our Daily Leper.
Victoria’s latest leper turns up in the per-
son of one Loono. For years the infected
pagan has been chief ironer and general rouse-
about at a laundry run by a shindykit of his
relations. Loong is 29 years of age, and,
according to expert evidence, has been diseased
for six or seven years at least, and his horrible
malady has been in active eruption for eight or
nine months. His “cousins” at the leprosy
emporium assume the stolidity and the dense
ignorance of a stone fence when questioned
about the matter; but it has been discovered
that since 1891 the contaminated foreigner has
been squirting water out of his baleful mouth
over the linen of a number of happy and
ignorant people who, in all probability, will
not sleep peacefully for a long time to come,
and will spend all their leisure miserably
examining themselves for rashes, and pimples,
and things, and sitting alone, feeling for
thickenings in their ear-lobes, and fearfully
searching their constitutions for “symptoms
akin to the above.” The discovery of Loong,
the leper, will do more towards the abolition of
the Chinese laundries in Victoria than a mile
and a half of newspaper argument, as the
person of cash and consequence will even
prefer rather to subsidise a large, coarse,
Irish widow, with 11 orphans and a great
want of proper reverence for the upper-
classes, than to clothe his fair daughter
in fine linen provided with germs from
the scaly hand of the diseased Hoo-Ki-La and
Co., and then live his days in an agony of
expectation, watching for the leprous fungus to
over-run the roses in her dimpled cheek. At
any rate, The Bulletin has no sym-
pathy to spare for any man who permits
the clothing of his family to be mauled by Hang
Oat, or any of his hundred million cousins,
should Hang prove to be a leper, and the
horror of it turn his white patrons grey
in a single night, and every pimple on their
progeny for 11 years become a source of hellish
anxiety, and ruinous bills from doctors and
apothecaries without end, amen 1 Australia
must decide that the leper brand won’t wash
clothes.
Garden-Party Government.
The National Review lately published a ser-
mon upon the duties and qualifications of
“ colonial ” Governors. The author described
himself as an ex-private secretary, who had
enjoyed three years’ experience with a Govern-
ment figurehead in Australia, and his ideas
concerning vice-royalty are full of melancholy
interest for the people of more than one Aus ■
tralian province at this moment. The first
qualification for success in the vice-regal billet,
says the ex-flunkey, is a title, richly gilded.
“ In Victoria the Governor will need to spend
from his private income quite .£6OOO a year ; in
New South Wales a private expenditure of
£4OOO will be found necessary ; in South Aus-
tralia, under the new or reduced salary, a
Governor will find himself some £2500 or more
out of pocket.” A wife of wondrous tact and
amiability is the second qualification. “ She is
looked to for guidance in all social and
charitable matters An insolent manner,
a lack of tact, discourtesy, or the merest
suspicion of parsimony in the dispensa-
tion of hospitalities at Government house
will render the occupants more unpopular than
a quarrel between the Governor and his Minis-
try.” A sublimely diplomatic aide-de-camp is
the third essential'qualification for a Brassey,
Hampden, or Buxton. As for the duties of a
Governor, they are expressed in the list of
qualifications. He must “ entertain ” lavishly,
dazzle sassiety with his titular distinctions,
have a dignified manner, and confine his
energies to the social circle. In short, he must
be the little god of a little clique of worshippers,
who devour refreshments on the vice-regal
premises and loyally demonstrate over him
elsewhere. The ex-Secretary’s artless sermon
is but an echo of Tory cant and newspaper
“ gags.” We have seen, in the case of Victoria,
how a Governor may be popular, in the
cant sense of the word, while his wife is
notoriously tactless, and, owing to her natural
diffidence, discourteous. Also, we have seen
that the unanimous absence in England
of three or four Governors’ wives made
no more difference to the public welfare than
the strange dearth of Governors during the last
few r months. And in this time of grovel-famine
vice-regal functions don’t entail a large expen-
diture from the performer’s private income, nor
necessitate a Hinglish title to his name. Vic-
toria enjoys seemingly the reputation (among
private secretaries and similar authorities on
the subject) of being the most expensive of Aus-
tralian provinces to govern, because “no less
than 5000 guests were invited to the principal
garden-party, and for a great part of the year
a dinner-party of some 40 guests was given once
a week.” This sounds like an absurd exaggera-
tion of fact, but for purposes of argument it may
be set down as a solemn truth. As a solemn
truth it has nothing whatever to do with a
Governor’s duties and qualifications. Granted
that as as 5000 people—the 40 weekly
diners being selected from the mob—have been
on the books at Melbourne Government House.
They represented about 1 per cent, of the adult
population. To the other 99 per cent, it
matters nothing whether the Governor spends
his own money, has a smiling wife, and belongs
to the peerage, or is the meanest, least-married,
and plainest mister under the sun. They recog-
nise, more or less, the official uses of a Governor.
He has power to prevent obviously disgraceful
legislation, which power serves as a check upon
political immorality ; and people who think
at all about the matter would prefer to have a
Governor elected by themselves as a sort of
national Speaker. They cherish no desire for
a Brassey ; they don’t order cannon-firing,
triurpphal-arches, and fiddledeedee on the occa-
sion of his arrival. A public holiday is thrust
upon them ; a show is provided ; they go to see
the fooling, some of them ; and that’s all about
it. Brassey’s reception is a necessary thing in
the eyes of sassiety, because sassiety expects
him to spend a lot of money beyond his salary,
but a need for his triumphal-arches and subse-
quent private expenditure doesn’t exist outside
the pestilential little world of grovel with itf
5000 unwholesome inhabitants.
Tasmania, where the ruling class objects to
straightforward payment of members, is a
veritable paradise of “ deadheads.” Every
Mayor or warden of every one-’possum-power
hamlet has the freedom of the post and tele-
graph service. The other day it transpired
that ball-invitations in a lady’s handwriting
had been franked through the post. At least
one legislator is known to have sent out all his
monthly business accounts “on the never.”
They don’t believe in adequate payment of
members in Tasmania. Every legislator has
free use of the railways and telegraph wires,
and letters addressed to members during session
go unstamped. The member is also privileged
to send reply free telegrams; not “ reply paid ”
thus: “Do you wish me to call on XXX?
Reply free.” Of course all distinguished
visitors get a free railway pass, and naturally a
good many who aren’t distinguished travel “ on
the nod.” In fact the railways might as well
be free altogether, and it would come cheaper
in the end. The Conservative papers have
always got along all right in regard to free rail-
way travelling, but the two Democratic journals,
though in existence for several years, have only
during the past month managed to secure a free
pass for their editors. And, undernocratically,
they accepted the privilege.
The gross Toryism of the Imperial Fitz Gibbon,
of Melb. Metropolitan Board of Works, exhi-
bited itself in his remarks re the sweating of
labourers by sewer contractors, an evil brought
under his notice by a deputation from the Anti-
Sweating League, the other day. Said the
Gibbon : —“ My Board has fixed a rate of pay for
labourers. The workman who accepts a lower
rate and then complains of the contractor is
guilty of dishonest conduct, and I will be no
party to paying him the balance he should
receive under the minimum rate, as such an aCt

A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It lias long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback- the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who makes
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.
would be giving a premium to dishonesty.”
Nothing about the premium to the dishonesty
of the bosses given by the Board when it refuses
to take measures that will abolish their sweat-
ing tactics. For the labourer guilty of “ dis-
honest conduct ” in taking less wages than he
should lawfully be paid, there is a good excuse :
he is between the devil, who offers him 3s. a
day, and the deep sea of hunger and destitu-
tion ; for the contractor there is no excuse
beyond the characteristic ravenous greed of
Fat Man. But in the eyes of men like the
Gibbon, of the Board of Works, the former is
the rascal, and the latter the gentleman of
business acting in pious conformity with the
holy principle of Supply and Demand.

The dramatic season at Sydney Lyceum
opened on Saturday witli “In Old Kentucky,” by
C. F. Dazey and Arthur Shirley. This play, as
the management explains, was never before pro-
duced in Australia, and The Bulletin regretfully
adds that it wouldn’t very much matter if it was
never produced there again. The drama is staged
with every possible attention to detail, and so far
it is a beautifully-finished work. The racehorses,
five of them, are realistic-looking animals, and
wholly unlike the second-rate cats-meat which so
often does duty in such cases; and of the twelve
people who make up the cast, four or five could
pass as stars in any high-class company. But
“ Old Kentucky ” itself is a sketchy concern which
drags heavily in places, and if the name of the
joint author had been Hazey it would have much
better expressed the situation. The first act is
full of go, but after that the piece tapers off badly.
The plot concerns a mountain girl whose father
was murdered many years ago by a bad individual
with an inward expression in his eye, and a noble
youth in the illicit-whisky trade, whose parent
was cut off by the same party. This murderous
person happens around in the first act —he never
arrives in any purposeful fashion, but just
happens around and his inward-looking eye
arouses dim recollections in both their minds.
Thereafter the story struggles along vaguely, till
the villain, by frequently happening around, has
tried to blow up Granville Blake witli a dynamite
bomb, and has set fire to the stable where Queen
Bess is located, and has attempted to lynch tho
youth in the unlawful-whisky trade. Then sud-
denly they remember where they saw thatinward-
lookingeye before, and theyoung man in the whisky
trade leads the owner of it by the hair into the next
room and bombards him to death with artillery.
Then he goes away to be tried anil released under
the First Offenders Act witli the approval, ex-
pressed in a private conversation only, of the
Attorney-General, ami no one knows exactly what
is going to become of him afterwards.
* • •
This little tale is garnished with niggers,
horses and bits of extraneous humor. Elton
makes a great deal of miithfulness out of a rather
thin part as a drinky colonel who has tried for 20
years to prdpose to Miss Nelly Mortyne, and that
pretty and clever lady climbs a tree with great
ability, and otherwise does a lot to fill the aching
void. King Hedley shows to much better advan-
tage titan he did in the horse-scene at Her
Majesty’s, and as a quiet, repressed, silently-
baleful sort of villain he is very effective. Scot
Inglis, though his ’Murken accept gives out at
times, makes a fine, upstanding young man in
the illicit-whisky trade ; and Miss Harrie Ireland
fills a small part and a beautiful dress to admira-
tion. Also, the new star. Miss Helen D-tuvray,
the “ Californian Diamond,” plays the part of an
energetic, wholesome mountain maid in a way
that endears her to the great soul of the audience,
and Granville Blake is a nice, colourless young
man, with an unexceptional hair parting. It is
vaguely understood that the latter gentleman _ is
ruined if his horse doesn’t win, but his financial
difficulties are very ill-defined. Tho racing scene
is not much above the average, and the burning
stable and the explosion are only average inci-
dents, and the fact that the inward-look’ng per-
son is killed in the next room is disappointing.
Even two rousing fights, in each of which the
illicit distiller severely disarranges his enemy,
have nothing specially new about them.
* * *
At Sydney Royal the geysers, and the hofc-
rprings, and the great Tarawera eruption con-
tinue to explode six nights in the week with great
i-olilt. For rich and picturesque scenery “ The
Land of the Moa ’’gets away from all competitors.
There is not one scene in all its stupendous length
which isn’t wildly unusual, and even the most
usual of them all would look extraordinary if it
wasn’t overshadowed by the more remarkable
nnustialness of the other sixteen or thereabouts.
The Maori war-dance, too, grows upon the public
with each repetition, and the maddened whirl of
these 40 chocolate-colored legs suggests brown imps
chasing a lost soul over the coals in a picturesque
hereafter. It is the biggest and maddest insur-
rection of brown legs ever seen on the stage.
Their color vaguely suggests that the figure of
Britannia on the back of the modern copper has
just broken loose from a lunatic asylum, and lost
most of her clothes as she got over the fence, and
their appalling velocity confuses the eye, until it
seems as if all the world was full of cataracts of
brown legs rushing down steep slopes into
nowhere, witli more and more and still more
brown legs to follow. They make a soulful and
refreshing spectacle, and whoever desires to fill
himself with brown legs should go to the Royal
at ouoe and be filled.
* * # *
The dreadful Greenwood family, which, by
the aid of Misses Maribel, Ruby, and several
other daughters, also Master Bob and the house
dawg, brought so many good dramas into dis-
repute a few years ago, has broken out afresh.
According to the pathetic story told in Sydney
police court, Mr. Greenwood has lately repaired
his shattered fortunes, and, in consequence, the
whole family determined to rush upon the stage
once more. “Leah, the Forsaken,” a drama
which is too old to defend itself, was selected as
the opening trouble, and the family wired £5 to
Mr. Greenaway, the well-known actor, on account
of salary, and cordially invited him to come along
and join in tho struggle. Greenaway, however
(The Bulletin is now telling that gentleman's
version of the story), hearing that the family was
breaking out again, and having been there before,
gathered up his clothes in affright and fled to
Sydney en route to an engagement with Plain Bill
Holloway in South Africa. Also, he kept the £6
on account of some arrears still due him from a
previous season with the same soulful combination.
Then Greenwood sent an extradition warrant after
him for embezzlement or homicidal vagrancy with
intent, or something, and Greenaway had to turn tip
along with the drunks and brutal-assault oases and
explain things. The explanation, by good luck,
proved satisfactory, and the extradition was re-
fused, and the Greenwood family will have to
break out by itself. Meanwhile “ Leah the For-
saken ” is und-rstood to be in active rehearsal, so,
presumably, Masser Bob is stage-managing in
place of the escaped Greenaway.
* * * *
Mahomet, the arithmetical horse, has
been putting up some new records doting the
week, and is more firmly established than ever as
the favorite attraction of Fitzgeralds’ allow. It is
confidently expected that this animal will shortly
add bookkeeping by double entry to his other
accomplishments, and then take a billot as an
auditor. At all events, The Bulletin is firmly
convinced that Mahomet knows more about
figures than many auditors of its acquaintance,
and if that honest-looking animal put his hoof
and attestation to a bank balance-sheet it would
be disposed to view the document with more con-
fidence than usual. The Roumanian lady and the
Professor drop from astonishing heights as usual.
The other night, after the latter gentleman had
fallen in with his accustomed regularity, a well-
known lawyer climbed up the ladder and looked
with a discriminating eye into the tank with the
severe expression of a man who was resolved to
investigate tilings by the roots. The fact that he
didn’t also fall into the tank was a severe disap-
pointment.

John Coleman, the first of Mr. Rickards'
new liatch of imported artists, has already estab-
lished himself as a favorite at Sydney Tivoli, and
proves to be a rather brilliant person with a good
many new ideas in his unpretentious head. He
is described on the bill as “one of the greatest
character comedians and expert dancers in the
world,” and so far he has lived sufficiently well
up to his character to pass muster. The Tivoli is
particularly well supplied with musical talent,
and there is material fora high-class concert com-
pany iu Harry Rickards’ show at present.

Lord Brassey and “ The Old Guard” put
in a joint appearance at Melbourne Princess’s last-
Saturday night. His Excellency achieved a more
distinct success than the opera, because bouquets
were provided for him by the management before
the curtain rose, and he was seen at his best
between the acts. Also the Legislature sat around
the new Governor, and tightened those “silken
bonds ” with an enthusiasm which no words can
express. The Bulletin can only remark “Rule
Britannia,” leaving the rest to its readers’ imagi-
nation. A revival of little Miss Flora Graupner
in her original character brought bygone memories
to bear upon “The Old Guard.” Flora’s natural
brevity is more noticeable than ever, after her pro-
longed absence from the stage, and now that
there is no Violet Varley, alas ! to keep her figure
in countenance, so to speak. But her nigh spirits
and broad smile fill the part very satisfactorily.
George Lauri, Miss Florence Young, Howard
Vernon, and Miss Ida Osborne do as they were
wont to do of yore. Mis Juliet Wray is a popular
Patatout, although the bloom of novelty has de-
parted from her. Wallace Brownlow’s appearance
as Marcel didn’t take place owing to a bad cold,
so Sydney Deane made gloomy music in his stead.
To-morrow (Friday) Lauri will take a benefit.

Six operas in six nights are promised for
the coming racing season. “ Ma Mie Rosette ” is
to be the Derby bill, followed by “ Madame
Angot,” “Old Guard,” “Dorothy,” “ Mascotte,”
and “ Yicar of Bray.” The combined effect of
these six successive shows upon the soul of a
sporting visitor who sees them all whilst under
the influence of holiday excitement is rather awful
to think about.

“Man to Man,” at Melb. Royal, is a
solemn melodramatic warning against the folly of
taking off your frock-coat in order to fight the
villain. George Rignold, who has had more
stolen papers thrust upon him than any three
other heroes in Australia, rashly flings his wed-
ding garment across a garden-seat at the bidding
of his bitterest foe, and is undone. A small boy
places the incriminating documents in a tail
pocket, where they are discovered by the lynx eye
of the law five minutes after George’s mar-
riage ceremony. For the next few years he
languishes in a convict suit, but at last
his frock-coat and fair young bride are restored
to him, together with all the earthly possessions
of his late employer, which had been made over to
the villain under a forged will. “ Man to Man ”is
brightened by the evil deeds of Augustus Glover
as a comic desperado, and the lurid, fitful presence
of Miss Maud Williamson in a skirt of mauiy
Miss Williamson is a gipsy having
secrets to impart, and Augustus brings timely
disaster to the night express, whereby the stage is
strewn with dying passengers, and proofs of the
hero’s innocence arise from the debris. Also the
engine bursts in a manner that ensures success for
the entertainment. Twenty characters, more or
less, mingle with the excellent scenery of “Man
to Man,” and tell the old old story for all it is
worth. It will run for more than a week, unless
George Rignold insists upon producing another
huge melodrama on Saturday. Rumours of
“ Youth ” are in the air.
** * *
A Grand Oriental Carnival, Fair and Art
Union will start at Melbourne Exhibition Build-
ing on Derby night (November 2), and pursue its
picturesque career until November 16. =£3ooo have
been invested for the benefit of this bold enter-
prise. The name of the things that will happen
during the fortnight is Legion. The Exhibition
interior is being rapidly converted into High-street,
Jerusalem, with gorgeous shops on either side,
and a colossal programme of amusements at the
far end. Concerts, variety shows, dives from the
dome, and larks with a live lion may be casually
mentioned as joys in store for visitors to that holy
city. Athletic sports are promised by way of out-
door tonic for the dazed and sated senses. One
will go down from Jerusalem to Jericho, as it
were, to see a bicycle race, and come back with a
renewed appetite for pleasure. The Oriental Car-
nival promises to boom. It reads like a dream of
Jerusalern up to date, and excites a certain feeling
of pity for Solomon in all his glory, who never had
half such a chance of being staggered at the cost of
one shilling.
** * *
The Alexandra Theatre has been awakened
from a long and dusty slumber by Charles
Holloway, and its doors will be thrown open to
the public on Saturday. The valour of plain
Bill’s younger brother deserves success, but The
Bulletin advises the management to be prepared
for the worst. And the worst —in the way of
Alexandra business —is a ghastly fate to look for-
ward to. “The Broad Arrow” will test the
situation.

Mr. Flavelle, erstwhile of the "Firm’s” opera co.,
better known on the field as “Ted, the actor," is running

tents of same have a depressing effect on him.

When Pollard’s Lilliputians were up North recently a
suckling dude promised one of the principal girls a floral
wreath. He spent a week’s pittance on a gorgeous mass
of flowers and sent it by a messenger to give to the door-
keeper. It chanced that an angular New Woman was
lecturing at the rival hall. The chuckle-headed messenger
delivered it to the wrong place, of course, and it was
carefully handed up to the Apostle of Cold Tea and
Liberty. Attached to it was a sugary note pleading for a
meeting and containing the sender’s name and address.
There was a sad five minutes interview the next morning
between a white-faced youth and a bard-featured female,
aged about 43.
Mias Leonore Orelli, whose fine voice is said to have
improved greatly during six months of calm retirement,
has been engaged for the Oriental Carnival at Melbourne
Exhibition building. The Jews heard Orelli sing on the
first night of the amateur " Pinafore ” performance, and
heard Flegeltaub Wray struggle with the same music al
hours later. After which they solaced themselves with
the assertion lhat the tall, fair damsel was really a Jewess
In disguise—which she certainly isn’t. All the same, the
Chosen will presenlly be able to claim that they "dis-
covered” and adopted Miss Orelli, unless the Gentiles
take her up.
The practice of changing the names of music hails is
becoming most bewildering in Melbourne, where each
house has been christened half-a-dozen times over. The
Cambridge becomes St. George's Hail again for Kennedy’s
Season. Each variety crowd that lands in applies a new
name, evidently with an idea of convincing the public
that they travel with their own hall.

Pascal, “ The Demon” of Fitzgeralds’ show, is a French-
man of sft. 4in., 14st. 121 b. weight, 50in. chest, 18J
biceps. He is the most solid contortionist alive—
quite an elephant doing the eel. His great feat of grip-
ping with bis teeth a mouth-piece set upon a table and
then poising himself upon it with feet upwards, took him
three years to learn and cost him two or three teeth. His
father w; s an acrobat, and Pascal began circus work
when four years old ; he has continued it for a quarter of
a century. Lein, “ the Crocodile,” with ids green coat
and sinuous twistings, never had an acrobat or such in
his family, but when he was four years old a circus-pro-
prietor diagnosed him as animated india-rubber and took
him in band. The Ciocodile is 23 years old, sft. 6in.,
13st. 41b. ;he is a German. BUh Demon and Crocodile
take eight hours sleep, and are very parlicular over diet.
Breakfast at 9 a.m. is a couple of raw eggs and a glass
of milk ; lunch at 12.30 cousists of, say, one chop, little
vegetable, a little pudding, and claret; nothing then till
midnight—after the show when a frugal supper is taken.
Fond or drink in the stomach during contortion acts
might mean choking. Both are very small eaters, believ-
ing, with Sandow, that to become strong a man must eat
little. Both avoid spirits and tea but each smokes about
12 cigarettes daily. The morning’s exercise is an hour or
two of limbering up, and the afternoon’s a steady three-
miles walk. Cold weather gives them nipping pains in
the shoulders and back ; they wear flannel round the
loins, and Use embrocations. But in warm wealher these
things are forgotten. Eight years ago the Crocodile put
his hip out contorting, and spent a few monthsin hospital.
One peculiar result of the contortionist’s woik is the
development of a solid lump of muscle, walnut-size, under
tile chin, which gives the wearer a somewhat extra-
ordinary appearance. Pasoal can speak English like a
John Bui’, and both he and Lelo talk English, French,
German, Spanish, and Italian.
Olga Nethersole lat°ly sprained her knee, bathed it in
Lourdes water, and, 10, it. got belter. Straightway 01 a
wrote a byst deal account of ibis miracle, and London
D.T. printed it. A patently, both Olga and the Telk-
graph were unaware that cold water from anywhere—
yea, even from the muddy stinkful Yarra—would have
done the deed quite as well. Cold water is tbecommonest
of all remedies for sprains. Many more Lourdes miracles
are of the same brand. The sort of people who beiievein
miracles are not over-addicted to washing.

Tuesday last (Oct. 29) was the centenary of the birth of
the poet Keats. Parson Walters and George Joseph
Rignold should lie interested in the following from Sidney
Ooloir’s life of the hard : “ A still jounger member of the
Keats circle was Charles Wells, afterwards author of
‘ Stories after Nature,’ and of t bat singular and strongly-
imagined Biblical drama, or dramatic poem, ‘ Joseph and
iiis Brethren,’ «hicti, having fallen dead in its own day, has
been resuscitated by a group,of poets and critics in outs.”
So that Rignold can’t fairly claim that the idea of a play
about Joe and Mrs. Potiphar was all ins own.
The cold-blooded Yic. authorities appear
nervous lest the. public suspicion that Emma
Williams is pregnant may save her from the hang-
man. At first the official doctors confined them-
selves to the opinion that the woman was nut
"quick with a quick thild ; ” upon pressure they
added that there was “no reliable evidence of
pregnancy at all.” But, as in the early stages of
conception, no absolutely reliable signs are
admitted by any authorities, this statement can
by no means be accepted as final. More-
over all agree that certain indications are
strong circumstantial evidence. These symptoms
are stated to exist in the case -of the condemned
woman, and it is likely that the public will insist
upon her getting the benefit of any doubt that
may be settled by development.
No woman should be held accountable with her
life for any act she may commit while pregnant.
A pregnant woman is not a responsible being.

THE CUP
WiLli be the leading topic during the next few
weeks. Will the new Governor eclipse all his pre-
decessors by the magnificence of his equipage and
the number of his suite ? Will the costumes recall
the bygonedaysof Victorian luxury, the subsequent
reactionary modesty, or will they strike the “golden
mean ?’’ Judging by the number of handsomecos-
tumes seen at Messrs. Manning and Co.’s, the Block,
Collins-st., Melb., the modes of the present year
will rival all their forerunners by their beauty,
taste and richness, while the prices will demon-
strate how far general values have fallen since the
plenteous period of the boom. The dresses of the
more recent Cup meetings have not been of a very
“solid” character; in this respect the present
year will mark a change ; and, should fine weather
prevail, the lawn on Cup-day will be radiant wuth
fair women whose charms are enhanced by their
sartorial settings. Head-dresses will be unusually
bright this season, and Messrs. Manning and Co.
(Coilins-et., Melb.) are making a gorgeous display
of fashionable styles to tempt the intercolonial
visitors who throng Melbourne during the annual
racing carnival.
A PUZZLE.
Readers of The Bulletin in the country are often
puzzled to know where to send their orders, so
many drapery firms are advertising what they can
do, but few care much how orders are executed.
There seems to be a general idea that “ anything
will do for the bush,” all forgetful of the fact that
ladies, even in the remotest backblocks, like to
have the latest styles and the freshest of goods.
Messrs. Riley Brothers, of 570 George-st., Sydney,
have for the past 30 years given special attention
to their Country Order Department, and know
intimately the requirements of every part of the
colony—North, South, or West. If the lady
readers of The Bulletin will look at this corner
of the paper every week they will see what we are
sending away every day. Gentlemen will find
articl.es.fot them in the corner of another page.

Baron Rothschild in TV. A.
It is the present intention of the Baron Alphonse
de Rothschild to pay a visit to Western Australia,
about Christmas time, in order to inspect the
numerous properties in which he has lately become
interested. Should this intention become an
accomplished fact it will be a splendid advertise-
ment for W. A. and her mines, and will bring a rush
of speculators who are only too glad to follow the
famous baron’s lead. His confidential agent has
been in the western colony for many months, and
has bought several valuable properties, and as
naturally we like to know all we can about a man
with Rothschild’s reputation, our correspondent
waited upon the agent and was graciously
accorded an interview. Our representative States
that from his conversation he gathered that the
baron is a generous man, who lives in princely
style and neither stints himself nor his employes.
He glories in a first-class smoke, and for that
reason indulges only in the B.L. Corto Roths-
childs. At Xmas time he presents to each of his
employes a box of these fragrant weeds. If some
of our colonial grandees were to follow his good
example and give their hard-worked clerks a box
of B.L. Corto Rothschilds at the'"festive season
they would get popularity at a trifling cost,
besides giving pleasure to the recipient. Con-
sidering that the smoke of princes, viz., the B.L,
Corto Rothschilds, are now obtainable at all first-
class tobacconists at Gd. each, and in boxes of 25
for 125., they are within reach of every lover of a
good weed.*
