846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney,
October 29, 1895.
Mr Dear Moorabinda—
Mark Hambourg farewelled to a matinee
stuffed with women on Tuesday of last week after
making a bright success of his Sydney season.
The audience looked as if each individual girl’s
main object was to catch the downcast eye of the
boy’s-size Paderewski, and the show of pink roses
and airy gimcracks was a compliment in itself. A
girl in the first gallery presented a very new effect.
She had on a greenish bodice and was standing
just behind one of those greenish pedestals which
divide the gallery-rails, and which reached to her
waist, so she looked exactly like a bust of a girl
of the period. Ladies who stand up in the Cen-
tenary hall gallery should remember that this
effect is lying in wait for them.
The programme was classic and affecting. One
maiden hauled off in tears, and another was sup-
ported out half-fainting, but no flowers were
thrown, and what hysterics there were appeared
to happen strictly in front. Mr. Sydney Muss,
Mr. Harry Weir, and Miss Blanche Thomas, a new
contralto, who wore white to suit her water-name
I suppose, helped to still further strengthen the
bill. I must say the audience listened breath-
lessly—partly for love of music and partly because
once the brilliant Russian ivory-pounder abruptly
left off playing and retired from the stage when
the crowd got too cacklesome, and only returned
when silence reigned. It wouldn’t be a bad idea
if mummers did the same at Lyceum Theatre first-
nights.
Kruse on Nov. 2 is the next musical sensation
to soothe the unsettled feelings of the people who
haven’t gone to see Melbourne Cup; and I hear
that the Marquardts are only waiting to get their
o.ir into musical matters. Madame M. harping
on her harp in her own angelic manner ought to
run to a successful return Sydney season in, say,
the Centenary Hall. Talking music, the most
amusing incident of the recent Hambourg season
was the way Australian pianists of prowess banked
their Promethean tires while the bow-legged
Russian genius was abroad. You couldn’t get a
piano-solo of consequence in any quarter where
his musical pyrotechnics were going strong, and,
although the stock-instrumentalists allowed their
names to appear on programmes, somebody always
arose before the item came on to explain that the
local genius had been obliged to go to bed owing
to that tired feeling, or something of the sort.
The unanimity with which everybody agreed to
let Hambourg have the platform to himself was
remarkable.
The newest thing I’ve heard lately is that the
warships on the Australian station are not going
to be ordered into active service in Hobson’s Bay
for Cup week. Just now, the navy seems to be
suffering a recovery from its long, long lapse into
hedonism, and the character it has earned in Aus-
tralia for chasing the glowing hours on largo tan
feet, and doing precious little else will have_ to
be contradicted, with apologies. The new Admiral
appears to be keeping up his character as a marti-
net, and promises ,to have even severer attacks in
future. For one thing, madame the Admiraless
is not coming out, after all, to figure as chatelaine
at Admiralty House, and the little Admiral, who
has hitherto given the necessary social attention
to the “shore people” in the shape of at homes
and tea-parties has, I hear, expressed his inten-
tion of letting society “go hang,” as they say in
“Madame Angot,” and only means to entertain
“his own ships,” when he comes across them.
But perhaps Rear - Admiral Bridge has a
smoke-concert soul and prefers cock - robin
functions, like the dinner the English ships
recently gave the Germans, while cruising amid
the coral isles. At this dinner a “ Yarman ” cap-
tain made the toast of the evening. Ho said,
“Gentlemans, I haf to you probose a German
toast. Herr captain, I drink your mozzer ; herr
lieutenant, I drink your vife and ze ozzer ladies.”
Who they were was fortunately not “ eggsblained.”
The pet of one of the warships at present on
Sydney station is called a Tanna hen, though it
isn’t a hen at all, but a rooster, in spite of which
the shorter name sticks. At mess sometimes the
bird is perched on a bracket and given fiery spirits
until intoxication sets in, and, as connoisseurs in
that sort of low comedy all agree that an inebriated
rooster can put more stagger into his stride than
any other member of the animal kingdom in his
cups, the sight is regarded as extremely humorous
by the naval heroes. Once this intemperate fowl
fell overboard as H.M.S. Pinafore was forging
ahead, and the ship was stopped and a boat was
lowered to rescue the feathered warbler. The
British navy is a most humane institution.
The navy ought to feel very tired —and so should
the army—when they see the crowds of people,
male and female, who wear army or navy colors
in their hatbands and neckties, whether they know
either profession when they see it or not. Since
that quartette of youths in “A Gaiety Girl”
sported bloodshot bands and scarves when war-
b'ing about the boys of the Household Brigade,
■ very counter-jumper does violence to the public
ye in liis haberdashery, and so does every subur-
ban girl too. So general have these hitherto-
exclusive badges become that the people who
really ought to wear’em are the only ones who
don’t.
The gee-gees will have left Australia ere these
scintillations get into print, and when the
shadowy distance blots out the myriad attractions
of tiie bubblesome co., people will console them-
selves with remembering that alter all pretty
Deoima Moore had about as much voice as would
be expected from a dotty bronchial tube; and
even the winsome Grace Palotta-took a No. 5 boot
and a glove to match; Also, that nobody ever saw
the real Maud Hobson, who was born with black
hair and the sort of complexion that usually goes
with it —for in Australia she was golden and
frescoed in many colours ; so what the real Maud
Hobson looks like will for ever remain a conjec-
ture. Ami people who incline to break their
hearts over Blanche Massey may find consolation
in recollecting that the beauty’s complexion, seen
at close range, suggested the “Before Taking”
picture on a patent pill advertisement ; and that
one at least of those fluffy little girls, who played
the society maidens, was at heart a downcast
hypochondriac perpetually “shaking the bottle.”
Ex-Premier Parkes got married again last
Thursday. Lady Parkes No. 3 (the daughter of
an Irish farmer) was a servant in the Parkes
menage for over four years, and was “greatly
attached ” tothelate Lady Parkes, who died happy
firmly convinced that her husband would , not
survive her three months ! The third ceremony
was performed at the Anglican church, Parra-
matta, with only two Parliamentary cronies of
the well-seasoned bridegroom (who must be letter-
perfect in his responses by now !) to support the
blushing pair, after which a bridal jaunt was
taken to the very suburb where Lady Parkes No.
2 has reposed in peace for the last three months
with a little glass lid in her coffin, put there to
enable her ex-husband to focus one last heart-
breaking look before the grave closed for ever.
The new Lady Parkes is said to be a fine figure of
a girl, twenty-three years of age.
The Royal Comic Opera Co. farewelled last
Thursday to a full and enthusiastic house, with
the usual number of bald-headed men glistening
like door-knobs all round the front row ; but then
the Lyceum’s speciality is the billiard-ball aspect
of the van when a show comes along with fleshings
in it. A few Saturdays ago I counted eight of
them (five of ’em almost egg-bald) in the front:
row, and a look round the dress-circle failed to
show any others so seriously affected by want of
hair. The man with the tennis-ball skull evi-
dently thinks that to be bald is to be beautiful, so
he loves to catch every ray of electric-light, firstly
on his shiny poll and then on his diamond stud. I
have often noticed and wondered why, when a
man’s hair has worn off on top, he should always
gravitate to the front row at the theatre (in the
audience I mean, of course, for if he was on the
boards he would most undoubted'y come next last
to the back cloth), and so I asked a leading Syd-
ney doctor to explain the whyness of the thing.
“Why,” I asked, “is it that the bald-head
is always in the front row?’’ “It’s being there
that m ikes him bald,” said the medicine-man.
By the way, what a golden age for doctors
is dawning behind the lady cyclists, and yet the
disinterested rnedicoes advise you please not to
buy a bike. Still, a craze is a craze, and not until
the shrieks of the wounded sound a loud warning
will women come back on to their own two feet or
their gee-gee’s four feet, and leave the wheel alone.
The mania is spreading in Sydney, where some-
body is busily getting a hall ready, cl la skating-
rink, where women will be taught to cycle on
hired machines. To do a tour is the lady-
wheelist’s noblest ambition, and yet I hear of a
well-advertised lady who goes from Dan to Beer-
sheba on her gridiron, returning from a very long
ride tanned and grilled almost to blackness, and
with the skin hanging off her in strips. So un-
sightly was she that she either avoided the towns
coming back or rushed them masked in a thick
veil that almost blinded her. If that sort of thing
is what women like, the sex must indeed be
evoluting.
No woman looks her best on a bike, but if it
comes to comparisons the she-Englishwoman gets
ahead of her Parisian rival, for the former clings
to skirts, which are the secret of woman’s great-
ness, while the latter leaves very little to the
imagination in boldly-bifurcated knickers. The
Americans think they strike a happy medium
with the baggy divided skirt, while in Australia
every woman appears to wear her own par-
ticular fancy, which is mostly knickerbockers.
Foolish virgins! “Skirts are to form what
rhythm is to poetry,” wrote somebody, I forget
who; and certainly the photos, one sees of
women skewered on bikes encourages the idea
that they look more like something new
for the Zoo, or promising subjects for a
comic song than seraphic creatures who ought to
be having sonnets written to their eyebrows, I
think—judging by her elegant appearance in Lord
Noel’s velvet suit —that Mrs. Brough would look
graceful on a cycle. But, then, how few Mrs,
Broughs there are, and how many short-legged,
fat women cyclists—suggesting that the egg-
beater is running away with the cottage loaf—
would we have to suffer in between ? By
the way, an official lady of Melbourne, who
has done much to encourage a love for wheels in
society, always has her photo, taken with her eyes
cast down. Prince Laurent, in the Mascotte,
would shudder to know the reason why.
“In Old Kentucky,” at Sydney Lyceum, is a
one-woman piece, and Miss Helen Davvray
(known to Australia as the “ Californian Dia-
mond” of years ago—so quite a J. L. Toole sorb
of audience turned up on Saturday night to renew
favorable impressions) is an actress who could puu
worse literary dill water than “Old Kentucky’
through. She reminded irte of a pared edition of
Maggie Moore, only that she appears to have no
vocal intervals like that other plump and pleasing
favorite. Miss D. Was a great hit as the warm-
hearted Madge, although her costumes were sim-
ple and phenomenally clean—her only sartorial
thrill being the jockey suit in which she suggested
that if the Ashland Cup was weight-for-age Miss
D.’s mount must have been the oldest gee in the
flutter,
Nellie Mortyne wore the handsomest frocks>
and her dressing would have weighed-in for draw
ing-roorn comedy. A brown dress she sported
was just the artistic tint of the bottle-brush
“shag” that bad little boys smoke as a pre-
liminary to baccy. Miss Mortyne goes on a sorb
of sartorial spree in “ Old Kentucky,” for she
has a new gown in every act. The hero
wore a brown suit in one act, with buff-
colored legs and white vest, in which he looked
like a sort of cock-sparrow in a pantomime, and
the villain erred in dressing himself so carelessly.
I have been brought up to think that no man can
plan or carry out a crime unless he is in full even-
ing dress, with a light topcoat and a cigarette, and
to find that evil may be wrought in a sac suit and a
felt hat such as the hero usually wears on his return
from trying to earn his living, is a revolutionary
notion. I liked the race-scene immensely—it was
the first stage-race I have ever witnessed which
wasn’t won in a trot.
It seems to me —re the absorbing topic of Bene-
dict Parkes and his new bride—that the Australian
Gladstone, in his extreme old age, has renounced
active politics and started in business as an incu-
bator. A wife can be replaced as easily as a
broken tea-cup* Women are queer creatures ;to
a man, it would be revolting to marry a toothless
old woman, and when he does (as ho sometimes
does), there’s always a flare-up and a separation
in short order; but reverse the sexes, and you’ll
find the rule does not apply to women. Indeed,
this ex-politician of 81 appears to be able
to bowl ’em over against heavy odds, for
he is said to have -cut out a young swain
in his recent marriage; There must be life
in the old dog yet, although the late Lady
Parkes was quite sure that grief at her loss would
kill her heart-broken husband in less than three
months—which shows the over-confidence of loving
Women ! But, after all, these things must be
looked at philosophically, and from the standpoint
of the eternal vital'principle, which here and
there asserts itself even to the verge of the grave.
The Grand Old Man of Botany Bay boasts that
he could have married cash had he chosen, and
for my part I am sure he could have had his pick
of the woman-suffragists, who always appear to
have a yearning to rest in his hair. But “as a
man of the people,” the G.C.M.G. wanted “a
wife of the people’’—and got her. It really does
look as if spouses grew on every bush for the per-
fervid admirer of Stella. The bride, by the way,
is Irish—which may lead to the Irish forgiving
their ancient enemy a great many things. This
latest Parkes wedding, by the way, has a most
amusing effect on the social status of the “lady-
ship” rank, which so many fat, political women
are a-hungering for.
Mark Hambourg must consider the music-fol-
lowing Australian female a weird product—•
though genius-worship is a form of feminine
hysteria all over the world. But here it was laid
on so wry thick. They all called him “Mark,”
and he retaliated by calling them by their
Christian names when he met them, though I
don’t suppose they would have permitted it had
they known that he had one whom he really, semi-
seriously admired—“ the prettiest and nicest girl
he had met in Australia,” so he said— and to whom
lie gave a brooch on leaving Sydney. His departure
for Melbourne was a spectacle. The station was
crowded with women, some of whom begged his
buttonholder as a souvenir. Mark refused on the
ground that it was a love-gift, whereupon it was
torn out of his coat and divided.
Apropos of music, I am glad to hear that Annie
Summerbelle’s well-written song, “Unrest,” has
reached its second edition.
Reprisals move very slowly, it would seem, in
religious circles. For instance, it was only the
other day that a picturesque Anglican canon gave
a Roland to Card. Moran’s Oliver re the alleged
spiritual cargo of rum on the mission ship John
Williams, though the Card.’s remark is blue-
mouldy by now. The canon alluded to the thumb-
screws and racks discovered in the foundered
Armada as “spiritual cargo;” and now, after a
few more months have passed away, the Kyardinal
will have doubtless got his little mediaeval quip
arranged to balance the canon’s. But the Angli-
can priest might have brought his retort more up
to date, by alluding to the “ holy Roman ” fancy
bazaar, held at a certain uncivilised N.S.W. town-
ship not so long ago, when some of the lottery
prizes were sundry cases of spirits, diced for
bottle by bottle by the local priest, in utter disre-
gard of the Gambling and Licensing Acts, not to
speak of the conventional religious scruples to
dice and grog as spiritual aids. Though 1 am
afraid the Episcopalian Church is not without its
faults in the same direction. What Frilz Rimma
would call a “bazaw,” which will shortly break
out in aid of a suburban Anglican church, has one
case of Australian wine on the manifest of the
gospel ship, to be raffled for per medium of the
snowball or the lucky-bag, or some such innocent
stepping-stone to the art of gambling. The
revenue officers should keep at least half an eye
on these pious fancy fairs.
Writes my Melb. correspondent:—
“ Ancient mariner Brassey took his “swearing-
in ” more calmly tlian his predecessor, and, when
the formalities were concluded, faced inspection
with folded arms and boots pressed down hard,
like a school-boy Casabianca rehearsing the burn-
ing-deck scene. Lady Brassey, too, kept cool and
examined the presentation-bouquet of Sydney
flannel-flowers and boronia (were there no Vic-
torian blossoms available ?) through up-lifted eye-
glass with at least a semblance of interest, which
was generally regarded as a healthy symptom.
To the very last, the ’augiity countess invariably
forgot to bestow a glance on the floral offerings of
the faithful, and once, at a society function, when
her son (gastronomically reckless like most juven-
iles) covertly filched and nibbled rose-leaves from
his mother’s posy, its owner was none the wiser.
“ Melbourne’s new vice-regal dame, who is tall
enough to take a bird’s-eye view pf hubby’s top-
hair, if required, saw the ‘'swearing-in ’ in pale-
grey crepon with white chiffon vest and touches
of magenta in her bonnet, while her shorter half
played his favorite role of ‘a mariner bold’ in
naval uniform and rather more sunburn than his
features could conveniently accommodate. With
him came Cant. Wallington, scarlet-clad as of old,
and attache Shaftesbury, round-faced and bullet-
headed, vide, Collins-street photos.
“Chief Justicess Madden faced the moment of
abdication with a smiling countenance, and ap-
parently tookcomfortin imparting to her neophyte
successor such useful wrinkles as the advisability
of frequent pivot-like revolutions to ‘ give ’em all
a show,’ and the value of ecstatic bouquet sniffing
as a yawn antidote, and so on. The dethroned
empress made her exit in the pensive toilette
which had chastened the opening of the Artists’
Carnival, her daughters counteracting the festive
effect of their big white sleeves by funereal black
stripes.
“Most people went to the opening of the Vic.
Artists’ Carnival, and found it a somewhat dis-
appointing spectacle, as stall-holders, fixings, and
surroundings generally proved to be own cousins
to those of the ordinary Philistine bazaar. The
artists, themselves, sounded the first chime in the
knell of expectation. Squat patriarchs and baldish
bachelors in floppy tail-coats and baggy boots were
cold comfort to those who reckoned upon velvet
jackets and flowing curls, not to speak of Bohe-
mian collars and taper fingers. Nevertheless,
after the first plunge, spectators opened their eyes
to the fact that bric-ikbrao, human and otherwise,
was not altogether absent. Melbourne’s alleged
“Beauty’’ really looked the part in a noble
Gainsborough toilette of pale-pink satin, her
soft golden hair, “rolled” in the fashion of the
period, crowned by a picturesquely-plumed hat.
The race-game, run by a society-syndicate m
powder and patches, was a centre of attraction,
thanks to the energy displayed by Speaker Berry’s
vivacious dark-eyed daughters, and the becoming
small-clothes of their male coadjutors. Mrs.
Baronet Clarke, amiable as ever, hawked round
the evergreen wax doll, and, in virtue of office,
wore a white frilled apron over her heliotrope
gown, as did tier colleague, a well-known military
daine, who otherwise affected the ugly ducklings
of her wardrobe.
“The opening ceremony was put through by
His Acting-Ex. and party, Miss Lucy Madden
looking fresh as a rose in a black hat and skirt,
worn with a soft yellow chiffon blouse, its sleeves
of a deeper shade, besprinkled with goldy-silk
pansies. The ct ing-Governoress, like every
second matron outside the frisky brigade, had
violets in her bonnet and a half-mourning gown
in stripes of black and white.
“The Quakers’ Stall was one of the carnival
disappointments. One reads of fair “Friends”
and pretty Puritans, but, unless the uniform of
the artists’ “ Shakeress ” is howling libel,
Miles Standish and friend Phineas were extra
susceptible, even for very good young men. The
much-vaunted “ dove-like-Quaker bonnet” turns
out to be a hideous grey hood, which disguises its
wearer as a “ Little Sister of the Poor,** or a Mrs.
Gamp, subject to ear-ache ; according as her figure
happens to be meagre or inclined to embonpoint.
Toe artists were in despair, at first, when they
found the carnival must discontinue for nearly
two days on account of the swearing-in. But
there’s a silver lining to every cloud, and, at the
much-abused function aforesaid, stallholders, who
were in the side-wings preparing to resume busi-
ness as soon as Gov. B. was off the premises, rose
to the occasion and did a brisk business in after-
noon-tea. The miracle of the loaves and fishes
was hardly more popular in its day than the
action of these smart girls in ministering (at a
profit, of course) to the wants of Friday’s parched
throng, chained to their chairs for fear of losing
them, from one till four, and frequently harrowed
up by false reports of the procession’s alleged
arrival.
Yours affectionately, Sappho Smith.

UNRIVALLED.
Society
The aggravating good humor of a Melb.
crowd under trying circumstances was again
shown on the occasion of the beaching of Brassey.
H.E. was advertised to arrive promptly at two,
and at 1.30 an enormous crowd had gathered
about St. Kilda esplanade and pier, but the pre-
liminary swilling and soapy gabble on the steamer
postponed the V.R. landing till about 3.15, and
during all that time the people stood around,
and baked in a sweltering sun. When the funeral
moved, the cheering along the line of march had
a feminine and juvenile ring in it, but, on the
whole, the people seemed glad to see H.E., and
willing to make the most of a gubernator who is a
solid man, and a out above the average grinning
figure-head sent along to “ govern ” “ our depen-
dencies.”
Inside the Exhibition Building, however, where
the favored 6000 who were “ class enough ”
to be officially invited to the swearing-in were
penned up for two hours, there was a very
obvious display of cultured disgust and yawning
ennui. It was felt that somebody was to blame
for keeping a whole raft of the very best people
gaoled up beyond the reach of drinks, to be
played to the verge of idiocy by a brass band,
so when, after many disappointments, the vice-
regal pair were led in, the upper circles main-
tained a certain hauteur that cast a chill over the
hasty proceedings. The cursing-in had lasted two
hours and a half, the swearing-in lasted ten
minutes ; then the Brasseys escaped, and the first
families slowly fought their way out, everybody
wondering how anybody else could have been such
a fool as to come.
The invited guests at the swearing-in of Gover-
nor Brassey—all high-nosed and imperious people
of ancient lineage—-shuddered visibly when Mrs.
Premier Turner tripped upon the platform and
presented Her Ex. with a bouquet. The proceed-
ing jarred on their highly-strung organisms.
Obviously, it was felt that the country had lost
prestige by trusting this small but important cere-
mony to a mere “Missis.” Any stamped-and-
registered “ Lydy ” would have done —the
Speaker’s “Lydy ” or the “Lydy ” of the lone
baronet. Why should not “our titled families’
be packed on top at show-time?
Driving back to Government House the Brasseys
had their first glimpse of Melbourne’s trouble.
Hitherto the extremely-poor had been lost sight
of behind the thick ranks of well-dressed and
apparently prosperous people; but between
Princes’ Bridge and the gates of the vice-regal
abode were gathered scores of miserable-looking,
ragged, poverty-stricken men, women and children,
arrayed there exactly as if spurred by a cynical
bitterness of spirit to get up a demonstration of
their own, and give their Excellencies a glimpse
of the gingerbread beneath the gilt.
Lord Brassey has aged considerably since his
last visit to Australia. Apparently all the por-
traits here were taken years ago. He is quite
grey, “ sideboards ” and all, and looks a hale man
of 60. On Friday he was sporting the corned-
beef complexion of a bluff, old sea-dog. There
was none of the paleness and languor of Hopetoun
about him, nor did he exhibit any of the young
Scotchman’s painful agitation ; he was cordial but
self-contained, and worked his gracious nod with
a due respect for the gubernatorial spine. As for
Lady 8., either that pleasant-looking young per-
sonage is a consummate actress, or she regarded
the whole shivoo as a most delightful picnic. She
laughed throughout the march round as if she
fully appreciated the joke, and realised that Mel-
bourne was being had on toast, as it would find
out when it came to know Thomas even half as
well as she did.
According to the papers, Premier Turner was
to ride in the same carriage with the Brasseys
in the procession, and when the head of the
Government was not visible in the shay all sorts
of thrilling rumors flew about, it being even
hinted that he was driven off at the point of Free-
man Thomas’s sword.
• • »
All the addresses read to Governor Brassey
by the local Mayors and other small fry had to
be submitted in advance to Private Secretary
Wallington, who remorselessly cut out great
pieces of bad grammar and bad grovel, and re-
fixed the spelling and punctuation, and generally
made the wretched documents look as publishable
ns he knew how. Generally, the lofty peroration
which had been fixed up by the united efforts of
the town-olerk and the inspector of nuisances was
sacrificed altogether; and the very long words
were excised for fear the Mayor should fall
oyer them. Then a clean copy of the re-
vised document was made out, and the Mayor
went out and learned it by heart in the
backyard, to the surprise and alarm of
the house oat, which spat at him and then fled
into the washhouse; and people looking over the
fence could see his washup in his shirt sleeves and
with his weskit unbuttoned declaiming to the
broom which stood up against the wall, and telling
it that its arrival marked an epoch in the history
of Victoria. Most of the local mayors in the last
few days before Brassey’s arrival took to firing off
the address of welcome in their sleep; and even as
they walked along the street in the day-time a
glazed expression would suddenly come over them,
and their thoughts would go far, far away, and
they would point a short, thick finger at the banana-
cart man and tell him that his presence was a
bright augury for the future of the country. If
Gov. B had been delayed a week longer, at least
four mayors would have gone into violent lunacy,
and read their address of welcome for the remain-
der of their lives in Yarra Bend.
• * *
Melb. sassiety scribblers have discovered
that the Hon. Miss Brassey has “ a beautiful
brass-mounted goat-cart,” intended to be drawn
by an educated “Billy.” After this announce-
ment, Vic. Parliamentarians will probably break
their necks in their hurry to repeal the local law
against working goats in harness.
• • •
The great Brassey grovel reached stupen-
dous proportions in the last day or two before that
hardy gubernatorial mariner reached Melbourne.
The Mayor and council of Warrnambool tele-
graphed to Brassey at Adelaide, asking him to
steer his yacht close inshore as he passed their
town so that he might hear them firing a salute of
welcome. The Portland councillors tried to go
one better by signalling some touching words of
welcome from the signal-station as the yacht went
by, but the use of the station was ruthlessly
denied them. So when Brassey’s vessel was about
due, in the dead waste and centre of the night,
they drove to Oape Nelson, and there on the wet
sands, among the seaweed and the crabs, and with
the midnight wind blowing the spray through
their whiskers, they solemnly welcomed the pass-
ing Governor by waving lamps at him. They
were seen, and lamps were waved in return, and
then they got back home again at 3 a.m. and were
happy. Also, some of them had a bad cold, and

Vice-Governor Madden was called upon,
in the absence of the sounding Brassey, to declare
the Melb. Artists’ Carnival duly opened, and
seized upon the opportunity to relieve his sur-
charged feelings with regard to the Gaiety Girls,
whom he declared to be creatures of loveliness
and light and joy, who devoted themselves to the
glorious work of elevating mankind and their own
heels at the same time, and who by their strenuous
efforts to make that carnival a success, and by
their beautiful presence on that auspicious occa-
sion. showed how devoted they were to Art, and
how anxious they were to exert themselves in a
good cause. At this stage a hoarse whisper was
borne to His Waxcellency to the effect that the
Gaiety girls were not in the building, that they
had never had anything whatever to do with the
show, and for heaven’s sake would His Magnifi-
cence switch off. Then Gov. Madden blushed a
beautiful shell-pink all over his regal baldness,
and coughed twice, and said that anyhow it was a
very nice carnival, and sat down, and tried to
hide his discomposure in his hat. Soon after-
wards a large, dignified gentleman of middle age,
disguised in a waxed moustache, was seen behind
a railway-truck on the wharf, surreptitiously
tearing the feeble remnants of a noble head of
hair, as the ship bearing away the Gaiety co. slid
over the horizon.
Gov. Madden’s absurd mistake was per-
haps due to his being misled by the powdered
surface of the stallkeepers at the Artists’ Carnival.
It wasn’t within the bounds of probability that J.
C. Williamson would let the Gee-Gees draw the
public to a rival show. But the Chief Justice
didn’t stop to think what he was saying. His
mind was perhaps fully occupied with the thought
of how vice-regal he looked when he was saying it.

It is rumored that Governor Brassey made
his triumphal entry into Victoria with the re-
mains of a very bad black eye, acquired through
falling against the binnacle or something one
dark night when the stormy winds did blow, and
chat his head was swollen and very tender owing
to a rope falling on it.

Another batch of (five) Victorian lady
missionaries has arrived at Sydney, en route
to China and Corea. The Bulletin advises
them, if they have any respect for the feelings of
their male white friends, never to stir out of doors
in the Chinese baokblocKs without carrying
poison. The most effective and portable preven-
tive to being hacked about by filthy Chinamen
while you are still alive is hydrocyanic acid—
packed in a homoeopathic medicine-vial, which
can be put into the mouth and cracked with the
teeth at the right time. It kills you instantly : a
friend of the present writer’s, who was dying by
inches of cancer, took it one evening and was dead
so quickly that he had not time to carry out his
intention of dropping the phial out of the window
at which he sat.
Thus Curzon in his “Problems of the Far
East ” :—“ A steamer rarely sails from American
shores for Yokohama without carrying a bevy of
young girls fresh from schoolroom and seminary,
who, with the impetuous innocence of youth, are
about to devote their young lives to what they
conceive to be the noblest of causes in Japan and
China. Only last year, at Kuei-hwa-chang, a
friend of mine encountered a missionary com-
munity of one male and twenty Swedish girls.
The society which iiad committed the outrage of
sending out these inhooent girls only allowed
them 200 dollars, or £27 10s. a year apiece, for
clothes and lodging.”

Victoria is making a “ record ” in the
matter of woman-hanging.
# # #
A holocaust of hanging at Cup-time. On
Monday, the day before, three people are to be
choked—Emma Williams, the poor woman who
(no doubt in a fit of madness) killed her child at
Melbourne, and two coloured murderers convicted
in Queensland. On the day after the Cup, Lynch
is to be hanged at Adelaide for the murder of his
nephew.
* * *
In barbarous Russia, the other day, the
authorities decided to hang eight men. Hanging
is a punishment rarely inflicted in the domains of
the Czar, being usually commuted to life-long
labor. To obtain a hangman for the occasion
official advts. were published far and wide, but
for weeks not a single volunteer responded, until
a solitary blacksmith offered to perform the duties
of public assassin. In Queensland, some years
ago, the hangmanship fell vacant, and nearly 200
applications for the billet came in out of a total
population of under 400,000. But, then, Russia is
a barbarous land.

* * *
London World is the “ aristocratic ”
weekly par excellence, and it is amusing to see how
it speaks of the class of peers to which Brassey
and Hampden belong. Sir Edward Malet, late
ambassador in Berlin, was lately offered a peer-
age. But by taking it he would have reduced, in
the order of Court precedence, his wife, who is the
daughter of a duke.
If (says the World) her husband were to be made a
peer, she would rank as his wife, and not as the daughter
of her father. This being so, Sir Edward Malet prefer!
that Lady Ermyntrude should retain the position which
is hers by right of birth, and not yield precedence to
Lady Tagrag, Lady Bobtail, and the other wives of the
bounder barons of recent times.
Delicious ! “ Bounder barons of recent times.”
“ Bounder Baron Brassey.” What a lovely even-
ing-paper thunderstriker!

Dean Stretch, of Adelaide, has been
orating on “Competiton,” and concludes joyfully
that “ the one and only check upon any unjust or
harsh operation of the law is to be found in reli-
gion.” And yet competition has operated to
, make parsons glad to go out in the howling
> wastes of Victoria and administer the consola-
u tions of religion to 50 scattered congregations
I at about 30s. a week. The parson’s solution of
J a social problem is always the same : If every-
thing were just as it should be, nothing would
be otherwise.
* # •
A most effective vote of censure has, in
the eternal fitness of thing, been passed on the
N.S.W. Crown Law Dept, by a common sweep.
The Bulletin, while in no way impugning the
good faith and scientific accuracy of official-
analyst Hamlet, recognises that he is only mortal,
like all other analysts, and that the prisoner
should have been represented at the official
chimney-sweeping and soot-analysing operations.
The trap laid for the analyst by the sweep, however
unsuccessful and unnecessary it may have been,
serves as a most suggestive protest against the one-
sided methods of Botany Bay criminal prosecution.
Under a fair legal system the Crown would have
tested the value of the public analyst’s evidence
in a manner similar to that in which the over-
loquacious and blundering sweep endeavored to
test it.

A PARKES PAEAN
Parkes airs his politics in private life,
For he believes in actual demonstration j
The other day he took another wife
To show his strong belief in Federation.
Thus Federation’s virtues are displayed,
And in a way to crtish the least objection ;
He holds it vastly better than Freetrade,
And evidently better than Protection.
His work for Federation has been great,
His enemies now dare not underrate it;
He wants to see Australia one state,
And also seems to want to populate it.
“This hinfant State ” —or words to that effect—
Has often been his phrase, and not amiss, too.
It must be owned, at least in this respect.
He’s done his best to make her what she is, too.
No doubt his foes his recent deed may note,
But let such carping critics still remember
To have the decency to not-to-quoto
That ancient gag about “May and December.”
Out here December is as hot as—well
A certain place that’s preached of by the
Primate,
While May is cold—so Parkes may safely tell
His critics that he represents the climate.

* # *
JJ-.S. Wales Crown Law Office unduly
“ rushed ” the latest trials of Dean. Prisoner was
arrested on Oct. 8, charged with making a false
declaration. Within 17 days he has been tried
before both the Police and Central Criminal
Courts. No matter what a man’s alleged crime,
he is'entitled to a deliberate hearing ; the practice
of committing an accused person from the Police
Court to a higher Court, sitting at the time, is
most dangerous and unjudicial, as in a sensational
case the excitement has no time to cool down, and
the jury are unconsciously influenced by one-sided
newspaper talk.
Salomons, Q. 0., has frequently expressed
his disapproval of committing prisoners straight-
way from lower Courts to sentencing Courts, as
the Crown officials are hurried in tiling true bills,
and the rush may result in a case being poorly
defended. (Witness the original Dean trial, which
commenced on April 4, prisoner being committed
at the police-court only eleven days previously.)
Chief Justice Darley and more than one of his
colleagues are also known to be very averse to the
practice.

* * *
Some of Dean’s defenders feel extremely
galled. “ I’m very sorry,” remarked one promin-
ent person, “ that I ever had anything to do with
the wretched business. Why, I took that man to
my house, introduced him to my wife and
daughters, and treated him just as a member of
the family. I shudder to think that we were
harboring a poisoner all the time. It might have
been worse, though. I’m not so sure that, if
Dean confessed to me, I wouldn’t have held the
secret. Then there’d be the devil to pay.”
A Richmond (Yic.) pawnbroker has altered
the title of his pop-shop to “The Brassey Loan
Office.” The “ Brassey Arms ” has been in exist-
ence for some weeks. Two steps already out of
the ghastly three. The third has yet to be taken.
Then Melbourne will rejoice in the possession of
“ The Bra§st-y Inebriate Retreat,” “ The Brassey
Lunatic Asylum,” “The Brassey Gaol,” and,
possibly, “ The Brassey Morgue.”

The Oriental Carnival, according to all
accounts, will blot the Victorian artists’ ditto
clean out of public recollection. The artists had
no funds to play with, and, although a mob of
enthusiasts were understood to be “working to
make the affair a great success,” their ideas on
the subject of show-manufacture were soft.
People who prattle art-jargon and supply little
sassiety-journals with paragraphs re their sub-
urban committee-meetings are soft, as a rule.
The Oriental Carnival, on the other hand, is a
Hebrew scheme, backed-up by hard-cash, and
arranged with a keen eye to pizness. The Israelite
at the head of it doesn’t look only to Jewish
sentiment for support, but invites the Gentile
world in general to pay a shilling for “the most
gigantic entertainment ever attempted at the
price.” Rather than run the risk of putting th e
artists up to a few wrinkles, the Oriental manager
was careful not to advertise the strength of hia
pn>grainme until the other show had fairly started.
You don’t catch an Israelite giving away anything
that a rival might profitjby. #
The feature of the Melb. Artists’ Carnival
was the four-horse drag traversing Collins-street
with a dozen Pierrots aboard.

» * *
The friendly societies which worked so
hard for the hospitals in Sydney Domain on Sun-
day week, might have more than quadrupled the
amount raised had they, like the Eight Hour
people, established a lottery in connection with
the movement. The Government which sanctions
a lottery—call it an art-union if you like—to swell
the funds of Labor processionists should tmcounter
no spiritual scruples in extending the official im-
primatur to a similar device for aiding such insti-
tutions as public hospitals. In France the hospi-
tals obtain a generous sum (say, iIIGO.OOO a year)
from a three per cent, tax imposed by Government
on the stakes in horse-racing.

The Melbourne authorities are silencing
the street-cries of fish-hawkers, news-boys and
race-card vendors. When the task has been
thoioughly accomplished, Melbourne will, by com-
parison, bo a city specially worth living in.

LOVE’S YOUNG DREAM.
A Chinese leper was recently arrested in a Melbourne
laundry, where he had been employed for years.
Wuu Lung, the flaky leper, by the laundry table
A fearful thing to look on, seamed and shrivelled
with disease,
And he mauls the snowy linen with his lean,
infected hands,
By day or night he labors as the master Chow
decrees.
He tills his mouth with water, and he lightly
blows a spray .
Oil that shirt-front most expansive, and his irons
swiftly go; . , . ..
For the owner, Percy Banker, is a bounder in his
way, ,
And his linen must be “ bang-up,” or there a
trouble, dontcherknow.
’Tis night, and in the moonlight where the stately
roses bow
Sit Percy and his darling girl, too full of bliss
to speak.
His arms are wound about her, and his lips are on
her brow.
And on his glossy shirt-front rests her dainty,
• damask cheek.

Readers of The Bulletin will recollect
some time ago a warm discussion was carried on
in these columns as to the respective advantages
of life in the bush and life in the city. As Sir
Roger de Coverley remarked on a famous
occasion: “There is much to be said on both
sides.” From a lady’s point of view one of the
keenest disadvantages of an existence in the
country is the difficulty she experiences in supply-
ing her wants in the way of ribbons, laces and the
thousand and one articles of feminine attire.
This difficulty, however, almost vanishes in face
of the fact that a twopenny stamp brings her
within reach of the vast resources of Mark Foy’s
establishment in Sydney.*

Visitors to the Spouting Spring at Heli-
don, 70 miles from Brisbane, are always astounded
at the quantity of water they can consume at the
Spring. Ten or 12 tumblers in an hour or so are
taken with comfort. The reason is that the
tissues of the body are absorbing the water all the
time. Something beneficial is always-added to
the system by every glass of Helidon Spa water
consumed. Messrs, Tooth and Co., Kent
Brewery, are the Sydney agents.

The Wild-Cat Column .
The National Bank of Australasia has arrived
at the natural result (anticipated by Tub Bulletin with
monotonous regularity for the last three half-years) of its
unwise anxiety to pay divs. and take little thought
for the morrow. For the last four half-years the
net profits have been respectively £31,312, £25,231,
£15,125, and (for the present half) £13,762, so that each
six months things have been worse than they were the
one before. This falling-off has continued despite a large
reduction in working expenses, which are now £lB,OOO a
year less that in ’93. In the face of this evidence of
trouble ahead the management has, ever since the recon-
struction, systematically paid away almost all its profits
in divs., and the amount thus spent in two and a half
years is no less than £86,813. No addition has been
made to reserve during this period, nor has any
special provision been made, so far as appears, for
possible depreciation in securities. Now it is an-
nounced that the whole of the existing reserve
(£100,000) will have to be carried to a fund for “the
equalisation of the dependencies of the bank,” which
seems to be a polysyllabic way of saying something
unpleasant. And in spite of this stupendous writing-off,
and the continuous fall in the profits, the 5 per cent. div.
on preference shares is paid as usual. It is about time
someone took the management aside and asked it, in a
serious tone of voice, where it thinks- it is going to.
Doubtless it is morally bound to pay its div. on preference
shares so long as the necessary amount is earned, but if
losses are not provided for out of the half-year’s takings,
then the alleged profit can’t be said to be earned in any
legitimate sense of the word.
• * • •
The National Bank has a magnificent business
Which, so far as can be judged, keeps up remarkably well
despite all trade fluctuations. Also, it has paid up the
stipulated 4J per cent, interest regularly ; and it has the
colossal cash reserve of £1,800,000, and another
£700,000 in Govt, debentures. Yet with all this mass of
coin earning hardly any profits it has paid off only
£500,000 of its reconstructed deposits in two years. On the
surface of things, the National should be in a posi-
tion to release one instalment at all events, and
thereby s ive a portion of the heavy outlay for in-
terest. The National, even in its present depressed state,
is in a much better situation than most Australian banks,
but considering its possibilities it is in a much w-orse state
than It ought to be, and the desire to pay divs. which
shouldn’t be paid, and to show sensational cash reserves,
are two of the causes of the trouble.
• * « •
The Colonial Bank of Australasia, which lately
obtained a reduction of interest on fixed deposits from 4J-
to 2J per cent., has closed its half-year with a profit of
£7BO. Last half-year showed a loss of £4694, so the posi-
tion has improved to the extent of £5474. The profit,
according to the directors’ report, would have been larger
but for two things : the reduction of interest was only in
force during a part of the half-year, and the expenses of
the second reconstruction had to be metoutof'the profits.
As the report doesn’t state the exact date at which the
new arrangement came into force, or what the aforesaid
expenses amounted to, it is impossible to tell with absolute
certainty whether the half-year just ended has been a
little better or a good deal worse than the previous one.
The figures, how-ever, look none too cheerful. For the
half-year ended 30th Sept., ’94, the Colonial made a gross
profit of £29,168. Next half, the gross profits dropped to
£18,791. This time they are £23,084. As the reduced
rate of interest was in force for at least three months of
the half-year, however, it seems that, under the old cir-
cumstances, the gross profits would have been only about
£16,000, which means that the fading-away process is still
going on. The expenses have been reduced over £lOOO
compared with the previous six months, and but for this
remorseless screwing-down there would have been another
loss, despite the reduction of deposit-interest. Within
the last 12 months expenses have been reduced at the
rate of £9OOO per annum, and still they have not been
able to keep pace with the falling-off in the profits. And
there must come a time when expenses can be reduced no
further.
When the Companies Act becomes law the
Colonial proposes to write off a portion of its capital to
provide for losses. No information is supplied as to the
probable amount of these losses, but the advances amount
to £2,650,000. If it were found necessary to write off 10
per cent, of this amount it would mop up half the ordinary
paid-up capital, while 20 per cent, would absorb the whole
of it. As the net profits, despite the reduction in
deposit-interest, are barely 4s. per cent, per annum
on capital, it seems reasonable to assume that a con-
siderable proportion of the investments are more
or less lost. At all events the advances, on a rough
estimate, and so far as the scanty information goes, seem
to yield about 4.) per cent, per annum all round, and as
no bank habitually lends out its money at 4J per
cent, this average appears to suggest that a good pro-
portion of the investments are either temporarily or per-
manently dead. The smallness of the returns is not
owing to any large amount of unprofitable coin on hand,
for the Colonial’s coin-reserve—though quite large enough
considering that it has just fixed up a boom-proof recon-
struction and can’t possibly be rushed by creditors—is
very nearly the smallest in Australasia. Next balance-
sheet, when there are no reconstruction expenses and the
reduced rate of deposit interest is in full operation,
should show an improvement, but though the Colonial
ought—considering its special advantages—to come out
all right in the end, there is nothing to whoop about in
its present circumstances.
* ♦ • •
The terms on which the B.N.Z. is to buy up the
Colonial, so far as the cable information goes, do not
seem calculated to make the latter institution's share-
holders feel good. The purchase price of the business is
nominally £461,211. Of this £133,906 is to be handed
over in cash, which means 13s. per share for the Colonial
proprietary. The remaining £327,305 is to be held pend-
ing the realisation of doubtful accounts, and the local
impression is that when the realisation is com-
pleted the shareholders will get out of this sum only
between £70,000 and £BO,OOO. Sundry alleged assets
which the B.N.Z. refuses to take over at all will probably
yield enough to make the total £1 2s. 6d. per share. This
means £225,000 rescued out of £465,000 of alleged capital
and reserve fund. If these calculations represent the true
state of affairs the Colonial is sacrificing a good deal to
get rid of its business and charging nothing for good-will,
and, considering that the business pays 6 per cent. divs.
on £400,000 capital, it seems to be going remarkably
cheap.
• * • ♦
Barnato’s South African bank, lately floated in
London, is a fine example of how money is made by the
mysterious process of sitting in an office and writing
things on a bit of paper. Barney Barnato, a retired acro-
bat, started the bunk and sold to himself 2,500,000 shares
at £1 each. Also he gave himself the option of taking up
the remaining million shares at £3 each. Then he sold a
lot of his own mining and other properties to the
bank, and the bank, which was exclusively himself,
made a big profit out of them, or at least Barnato,
who was the whole management and directorate, said
it did. So far the bank consisted of Barnato selling
his own shares to himself, and buying his own mines from
himself, and paying himself a big div. out of the profits.
But now he sold 500,000 of his shares to a syndicate at £2
each. Then he began to sell a few to the public at £3
each, and finally he bumped great masses of scrip into the
London market, and, at latest, the public was taking all
it could get at £4 10s., just 4J times as much as Barnato
paid himself for them a year or two ago. And yet nobody
except Barnato himself seems to know anything about
the bank, and nobody seems to know much about
Barnato, save that he is a retired circus-tumbler who has
struck it rich at Johannesburg.

The United Insurance Op. maintains its reputa-

and marine premiums and interest for the year ended 30th

124 per cent. div. absorbs £'8528! £750 goes to reserve,

furniture i and £9214 is carried forward. The United In-

any other that Tim Bulletin is acquainted with, and
when the reserve-fund and the re insurance reserve-fund
and the reserve for unadjusted losses and the reserve for

A- railways last year yielded a profit of £‘d
28. 6d. percent., which is a big drop from the£3 10s. 9d. of
the previous year. As the money for railway construc-
tion was borrowed at a shade over 4 per cent., this means
that the railway deficit has suddenly gone up about
£50,000. The falling off in the Broken Hill traffic
has knocked the bottom out of the S.A. rail-
way business. Despite the addition of 58 miles of new
line, the receipts have dropped nearly £40,000 as com-
pared with last year, and the profits have declined £39,000.
Westralia is now the only Australasian province which
nasn t a heavy railway deficit.

“During the last five years,” says Brisbane
Courier, “ £19,000,000 has been added to the railway
debt of Australia, involving an addition to the annual
interest hill of £660,000 a year, while the gross railway
earnings have increased by only £51,599.” Almost ail
railways which the present population could find work
for were built by 1890, and the lines constructed since
then have, as a rule, paid neither interest nor axle-grease.
Yet Queensland, which has already more railway in pro-
portion to population than any other country on earth,
except, perhaps, W.A., and the worst railway deficit on
this continent, is going to construct ten more lines
shortly. But, as Premier Nelson says, “ the compensa-
"ol> l wlH ” e heavy’’- and some influential people want
that heavy compensation very badly indeed.

Twice last week, in N.S.W. District Court,
Judge Murray fixed 8 per cent, per an. as the legal
interest on money due. One of the cases was a little
remarkable. A financier claimed £69 10s. for money lent,
but adding to this sum the interest accruing in 13 months
he made out a gross claim for £246 14s. 6d. Compassion
for the debtor, or a desire to bring himself within the
of District Court law, induced him to knock off
-650 I make a present of it,” he said—thus reducing
the amount of claim to £196145. 6d. Judge Murray gave
a verdict for £69 10s., and awarded interest at the rate of
8 per cent, per an. for the 13 months-or about £75 in all.
8 per cent, may he a desirable figure, hut why does the
law permit each Judge to establish a figure of his own?

day advertised for a man to run his paper—keep the

The want of sufficient water minimises the use
of the Hillgrove electric-power scheme. It has been
found to run smoothly, is easily handled, and gives
general satisfaction. With anything like an average rain-
fall 1000 horse-power can be developed.
• • • *
Pay-day is a heart-breaking time for many a
Vic. boot manufacturer. The wholesale leather merchants
worry over the fact that some of their customers, doing a
big output, continually require assistance with their bills
In fact, a clear sheet never happens. Competition is
down beluw bed-rock, profits cannot be made to provide
more than rent and the bosses’ expenses, so the hands are
paid out of the sales of portions of the stock. Retailers
know this—hence Thursday’s and Friday’s sales are respon-
sible for the employes getting their screws on Saturday.
♦♦* * J '
Stories of the ancient smash of a certain
Westerly bank are continually cropping up. Here is
One. A trader owed one of the directors a considerable
sura—thousands—for which the director pressed him
sorely. A little time before the collapse the trader
decided to “go broke," but first proceeded to the capital
to make a last appeal to his chief creditor. The latter
advised him to borrow £lO,OOO or so and start afresh
“But where am I to raise such a sum?” asked the trader’
“ Now, listen to me," quoth the D. “The bank is doing
a roaring trade just now—lately declared a rousing div.—
go and state your case ; go to-morrow morning. They’ll
give you an overdraft; have no misgivings about that."
Acting on this excellent advice, the trader saw the man-
ager, got an overdraft of £9OOO, paid the director
bought a fresh lot of stock with the balance, and went on
his way rejoicing. He sold most of the stock at a good
profit, with every probability of clearing the lot when
the bank went bung. So did the trader. The banfc col-
lared the balance of the stock, and the director winks his
eye when he meets the man who paid him.

* * • ♦
A Book Debts’ Bill is being put through Vie
Council by Sargood, who testifies feelingly to the woe of
warehousemen on discovering that an insolvent customer
has privately disposed of his outstanding accounts to a
pal. The Bill in question will put book-debts under the
same commercial law as stock assets, which can’t be
pawned without an official registration of the fact that
they have ascended the spout. Under present Smell-
bourne condition no man knoweth to whom he is indebted
for his milk arrears, or money due upon the clothes he
stands up in,

else there are so many notoriously-cronk influential citi-
zens tlicvt the authorities Uaren’t) run the risk of spotting
their little delinquencies,

That big brewing concern, Tooth and Co., Ltd,,
of Sydney, has a net profit of £43,048 for the year
ended 30th Sept. This amount includes last half-year's
balance, and makes liberal allowances for all kinds of con-
tingencies. A 7 p.o. per annum div. absorbs £81,500 ;
£5OOO will go to reserve (now £55,000); £3OOO is allowed
for machinery depreciation, &o. ; and the balance, £8546,
starts tlie new half.

The coy bank-note is beginning to make its
appearance about Melbourne again. Since the beam ex-
ploded brand-new sovereigns have been the favorite
medium, but just now there is quite a quantity of dirty
and disreputable-looking pound-notes moving about. lu
consequence of reading, a few years ago, that a Chinese
leper arrested at duties was using a wad of pound-notes
for a liver-pad, or chest-protector, the writer has always
taken it in gold, thanks.
• ♦ • $
On July 21 the fire broke out in Block 11 of
B.H. Proprietary. On Oct. 21 (just three months after-
wards) it first appeared in Block 10, the mine to the
south. At Block 10, threatened from the first, elaborate
precautions were taken ; courses of timber were removed,
and the spaces filled solidly with non-combustit)le tail-
ings, which, with the standing ore and rock, were thought
to thus form an impassable wall near tire boundary. As
the fire in Block 11 smouldered—and only a fortnight
ago Tins Bulletin stated that it still smouldered, and
that the position had never been more serious—and as
heavy blasting was done almost over the charred timbers,
subsidences, of course, took place ; and, as the result of
one of these, a fissure was probably formed, acting as a
conduit between the workings of the two mines. The
opening of the shafts near the affected ground in Block 11
supplied air at one end ; the fissure and an old mullock
pass in Block 10 supplied the outlet; and thus a good
draught was caused. How intensely heated was the air
which rushed out of the pass may be judged by the fact
that the timbers near the surface caught fire, though
there was not a stick of wood, and therefore not a spark
of fire, lor a depth of 150 feet below these few surface
pieces.
Firemen were able to at once go down Block 10
mine and approach to within a few yards of the burning
timbers. The sight was magnificent: there was no mere
smouldering here. In Block 11, it will be remembered,
the men could never fight the fire at close quarters—first
on account of the heavy smoke, then on account of the
ground being so broken. But there was a better draught
here, and the smoke was carried away ; also, the ground
was better stayed by pillars of ore and filled-in spaces.
Still, the firemen’s job is not a pleasant one ; and it is
not unusual for a man or two to be brought to the surface
overcome, either by smoke or the gases which are given
off. b
The great advantage Block 10 mine possesses
over Block 11 of the Proprietary mine is this; it has no
huge surface excavations, reaching down below the upper
timbers, and thus forming an inlet for air and an outlet
tor smoke. When the square-set system of timbering
with Oregon was introduced by ex-general manager
Patton, he never contemplated that the timbers would
be exposed like tins; and now that they are exposed,
and the whole conditions altered, it becomes urgently
necessary to consider whether the square-set system or
soft wood is still the best.
Capt. Thomas, underground manager of Block
10, is credited with saying that “for weeks past he had
expected the fire to reach Block JO ; and when he heard
last week that Block 11 was to be opened up he knew
they would have it quickly.” He also speaks of “ a mine
that caught fire in the Burra, S. A., 38 years ago, and
burned for two years. It was opened up at the end of 12
months, but had to be shut down again, as the fire was
as bad as ever.” A pleasant prospect for Proprietary
shareholders, at any rate, seeing that Block 11 is still the
best part of the mine.
Next the Block 10 is the Central; but that is
safe.

B.H. Junction half-yearly report (to Sept. 30)
is the most satisfactory docuraeut issued by this 00. since
that great and good man, Jolly, reigned there The
directors now, in view of the development off M‘lntyre’s
shaft, speak of the “practically unlimited” supplies of
sulphides. The directors, it seems, have two proposals
under consideration. The Camden syndicate first w-
gestedabig sale of the ore ; then, having (so the directors
say), secured 40,000 Junction shares, proposed the recon-
struction of the company, “ with a duplication of the
capital and the establishment of a London register ”
The latter proposal was made after the M’lntyre develop-
ment ; further particulars are awaited by the directors.
Junction sulphides are fairly rich in both silver
and leadf While the ore of, say, the South mine is worth
about Boz. silver and 60 per cent. lead, this is worth on
the average 25 to 30oz. silver, and 30 to 40 per cent. lead.
The ore from the M‘lntyre development goes about 30 to
350 z. silver, and 35 per cent. lead.
A start has been made with the new concentrat-
ing plant (Hancock’s patent) for the British B.H This
mine is now supplying a small quantity of sulphides to
Block 14. By the way, the weekly returns supplied by
the latter 00. would be much more valuable if it was
shown what quantity of its own and what of bought ore
had been concentrated. Also, “ the usual fair assav for
silver and lead ” is hardly a satisfactory statement of the
result of the work of concentrating ; but no other is given '
to shareholders and public.

In the vicinity of Barraba (N.S.W.) some rich
patch orushings have lately been put through. The best
claim is known as the “ Dodger.” Three tons gave 560 z.
to the ton, and the average of several is over 20oz. The
claims are on private property. The formation is largely
serpentine, and, as usual with such a formation the
richest gold is in oaicite. A little three-head battery
some miles away does crushing duty for the crowd.

According to Inverell Argus, “a load and a
half of wash-dirt from the new shaft at Aitkens, Dasey
and party’s diamond and tin claim, near Boggy Camp
N.S.W., yielded 515 diamonds, weighing 184 carats. The
stones are a splendid sample, many of them weighing 11
carats each. This was not picked dirt, but was put
through the machine as it came from the mine. This
claim, known as the ‘Scar of the South,* is the pros-
pectors* claim on the new diamond find, and is held by a
party of four. There are several other parties at work
hoping to bottom on diamond dirt.” “Many of them U
carats each! ” Granted the truth of this, and that the
stones are of decent quality, the holders have got a hi"
thing. And so has N.S. W. “

Some Southern boomers have an eye on the
Hodgkinson (N.Q.) goldfield. Men are engaged to take
up and hold ground abandoned for years past, and which
can be had for nothing; this will no'doubt be traded off at
extravagant figures in the Southern and English markets.
The Hodgkinson is a good field but undeveloped, and it is
unfair to make the public pay for mines which can be got
for “ nix.” Intending investors who will visit the Hodg-
kinsou can get excellent shows for next to nothing.

* * * *
A Coolgardie wire reports the closing of the
Boulder and Lake View batteries for want of water. “At
Hannan’s, water ia very scarce, quantities being brought
in from White Feather. Frequently the hotels can supply
nothing but raw whisky, not even beer, and no tea is
provided at meals. People are rushing into Coolgardie,
where water is plentiful. The residents are execrating
the Govt., and praying for a big thunderstorm.” Less
than a month ago it was understood in the East that
there was plenty water on the field. The rains this winter
were above the known average; scores of shafts have
been sunk below water-level, and, so far, batteries do not
consume one-tenth the water required on any permanent
goldfield. Millions subscribed in London long ago to find
gold ; nolhingfor water. It looks as if this summer the Cool-
gardie water-difficulty will be worse than ever, and that
fever will secure hundreds of victims. Tens of thousands
of people in a waterless waste is serious business for the
W.A, Govt.

*- * * *
Bayley’s last crushing went a little over 7dwt.
At the half-yearly meeting last week the directors
regretted many things; mostly the want of gold, and
consequently, absence of divs. No important discoveries
have been made. “The scarcity of water continues a
source of difficulty and expense. Six acres of land have
been purchased 12 miles from Perth. This is in every
way suitable for a battery-site, and gives ample space for
cyanide works in case it is decided to erect them later
on.” It was also decided that in future only payable
stone should be put through the battery; that further
exploration work should be done, and that part of the
battery, not required should be thrown open for public
use. This is the first intimation we have read of the
existence of non-payable stone in Bayley’s. This mine
has a market value to-day of over £200,000, while its real
value is probably nearer as many shillings—possibly not
as many pence. Many people bought at £1 since the iaso
To-day.there are tens of thousands of shares ov*r-
sold, and it is only when the bears are covered that the
market will expose its real value.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Premier Kingston is searching for eligible
Liberals with whom to fight Conservative consti-
tuencies next year.
Neild, M.L.A. (N.S.W.): “At the pre-
sent time an M.P. is simply a perambulating
labor bureau, and his life is a sorrow to him from
daylight till dark by reason of political patron-
age.”
A thing to be placed to the credit of the
Reid Govt, is that, defying the most honored of
Botany Bay Executive traditions, it has never
yet disregarded a jury’s recommendation to mercy
in a capital case.
Murray Smith, the Yic. Freetrade M.L.A,,
objects to the suppression of sweating by Act of
Parliament. It should be put down, he thinks, by
the force of public opinion. Query : When did
the mere force of public opinion put down any-
thing?
It is pointed out that the Victorian
Speaker now only gets£looo a-year, and the chair-
man of Assembly Committees £4OO, whereas the
N.S.W. Speaker gets £l5OO and the Chairman
£BOO. N.S. Wales has not yet seriously begun to
retrench.
Gem from the wisdom of Morrison re
Chinamen : “ With regard to the competition
between the European and Chinese in the labor-
market the former must inevitably succumb. Of
two currencies in a country the baser will always
supplant the better.”
It has the appearance of Liberal progress,
anyhow. Last week N.S.W. Leg. Council passed
the second reading of Reid’s Land-tax Assess-
ment Bill by 30 to 11. In ’B6 the same Council
rejected the second reading of the Jennings
Land-Tax Bill by 23 to 7, although the Reid Bill
means a tax of Id. in the £, whereas the Jennings
Bill only claimed a half-penny.
Dr. Bowker, M.L.C., the most inveterate
opponent of all new political ideas, is now of one
age with Parkes, and began work on his own
account early in his teens. He has been working
ever since, and even now earns his week’s income
by personal industry. When he rises in N.S. W.
Council to speak in the interests of property his
cry is as that of a man in the grip of garrotters.
Just before his death, the late Barrister
Healy—in his day, perhaps, the foremost N.S.W.
expert in criminal law—was engaged in reviewing
the Mount Rennie case at the instance of the
Crown Law Department. The Bulletin under-
stands that Mr. Healy had got far enough into
the matter to see that the surviving prisoners
should be released. Will some M.P. please ques-
tion Ablepharus Gouldi in this matter?

-—One of Thru.
One clause of Minister Peacock’s Yic.
Factory Bill provides that no factory or work-
room where any Chinaman is employed shall be
used as a sleeping place under pain of a heavy
fine. J. Beef Patterson complains that the clause
is dangerous and tyrannical. “ What is to be
done,” asked the butcher of Oastlemaine, “ when
a Chinaman falls asleep over his work ? ” “ Wake
him up,” answered the brief and practical
Peacock.
Governor Braasey’s “ only desire in leaving
his home in England and coming to Victoria was
that he might assist in strengthening the bond
between the colonies and the mother-country.”
The 4 per cent. bond.
A terrible fear besets the Fat Men of
Oentralia lest the Nelson Govt, be ousted and its
place supplied by a Liberal-cim-Labor party.
Rather than see this they would sacrifice all they
once held dear—even Separation itself. The
Hughenden-Winton extension spells disaster to
Rockhampton, but her legislators would rather
see it a thousand times over than be the means of
upsetting the Boodlewraith and helping in the
party of Reform.
The bill providing that every domestic
Biddy in Maoriiand shall have a weekly half-
holiday, and that if she is not driven out, in the
rain if necessary, to have her half-holiday she shall
be deemed to have been unlawfully kept in by her
mistress, or something to that effect, went over-
board the other day, among the unfinished busi-
ness of the session. Premier Seddon, who is
growing old with the incubus of much Biddy
legislation, was probably glad to see it.
One week, Premier Nelson would hear of
no attempt to shelve the Winton railway. Next
week, he allows a committee to consider the whole
question. Flabbiness or finesse; perhaps both.
North and Centre fight over Winton like dogs
over a bone, and the South can’t settle which side
to favor. New railways soon demoralise a House.
It is easy for an erstwhile honest man to persuade
himself that a line through his constituency would
pay well, however other lines might fare.
Premier Kingston objects to the admission
of Chinese into Australia, but protests against
those who are here being “made slaves of.”
This is just the root of the whole Chinese diffi-
culty. If “ those who are here ” would live and
work like free, self-respecting heathens there
would be little to say against them. But they are
not built that way. If the Chow is not made a
slave of by some richer Chow, or Chow co., he
will make a slave of himself on his own account.
It is his nature —bred in the bone of him for thou-
sands of grimy years.
When the bill for placing the N.S.W.
Civil Service under the control of commissioners
came before the Assembly, the old political
jobbers rose as one man, and moved that Parlia-
mentary officers, employes in the museum and art
galleries, the police force, the whole Education
Department and the Public Works Department
should be left under political rule as heretofore.
If they had succeeded they would probably have
gone on to exempt most of the other departments.
The old longing to “app’int Maloney” is still
strong in the politician’s soul.
J. L. Fegan, M.L.A., compares the pre-
sent N.S.W. Assembly to a temperance-lodge in
point of sobriety. This is one of the improve-
ments for which payment of members is directly
answerable. In days when men simply went into
the Legislature for “ honor and glory ” there was
little responsibility, less work (unless by pro-
minent office-barraokers), and endless refreshment-
room bibbing, to which some of the cleverest suc-
cumbed. Now the Parliamentarian has at least
to make a show of earning his screw. This
is a hard point for salary-hating Anxious Cameron.

WHY SYDNEY CITY COUNCIL OBJECTS TO SIGNBOARDS.
The new British Tory Government is pre-
senting ultimatums in all directions. One has
been served on the black monarch of Ashantee,
who is to be annexed at an early date, and a par-
ticularly fierce ultimatum has been sent in to the
republic of Venezuela. The border-line of Vene-
zuela and British Guiana was always an unfixed
and dubious affair, but, a few years ago, a gold-
field was discovered on the debateable land between
the two States, and Britain promptly settled the
difficulty by annexing all the known gold-bearing
country and fixing the frontier as she darned well
pleased. Some officials who were found m the
Venezuelan end of the doubtful territory were un-
hesitatingly “chucked out,” and now the little
republic is warned that it must agree to the new
arrangement at once, or it will be disembowelled
and its remains dragged all over South America.
Why, The Bulletin again asks, should
N.S.W. (the only country in this hemisphere to
do so) accord free-postage to newspapers, con-
sidering the tremendous loss on her postal °P er^:
tions? It isn’t that the newspapers can t afford
to pay postage, seeing that the price of web-
printing paper is now 50 per cent, less than it was

institution of oompletely-free State education the
other day, on the ground that the province could
not afford it 1
Maorilaud Parliament having gob to that
stage of weariness when it is ready to throw the
whole country overboard in order to tinish the
session and get home, thirty-one bills were oast
out the other day in one act. The Referendum,
the Betterment Bill, the abolition of capital
punishment, the new Local Government system,
and the scheme for municipal fire insurance were
among the derelicts, which represented seven
weeks’ hard work sacrificed. Every year every
Australian Parliament winds up by a general
throwing overboard of half the unfinished work of
the session in sheer disgust and weariness, and
then it has to be all done over again, and thrown
overboard again in a half-finished state next
session, and so on ad infinitum. Half the un-
limited cackle of Parliament would be saved if
the unfinished business were taken up again next
session just where it left off, but no Legislature
has yet had sense enough to effect this very simple
and obvious reform.
Vic. Coroner YouTs guiding maxim :
“ There’s nothing so deceptive as eyesight, except
hearing.”
Professor Loisette is the man who delivered
a lecture on Memory at an American ladies’
school, and went away forgetting his umbrella.
In a Maoriiand city, Talmage’s remarks
were gibbered at by a bibulous stallite. A big
laugh resulted when, as the offender was being
passed outwards, he yelled, “ Yah, gar’n yer ole
Tui!” —Tui being Maoriland’s parson-bird or
magpie, a bird with jet-black plumes and a small
white-feathered necktie.
A wealthy 82-year-old identity, living in
retirement on a bay off Sydney Harbor, boasts
that though over 60 years in Sydney he has never
travelled further than Parramatta. The veteran
does not use the ferries, but rows over daily in his
own boat to parade streets which, when he first
saw them, were little better than sand-tracks.
There is only one officer in N.S.W. detec-
tive force who goes in for disguises. Ordinarily,
he looks the typical Yankee detective —clean-
shaven, with a brown-haired wig, soft hat, frock-
coat, and light-checked trousers —his occupation
can be told a mile away. When “made-up”—in
that way he does almost all his work —even his
closest acquaintances and brother officers do not
know him till he reveals his identity. His
favorite character, when hunting for information,
is that of a talkative Irishwoman, although he
occasionally bursts out as a well-to-do squatter or
swaggering bushman.
J. R. Houlding, “Old Boomerang,” ia
well on with two new novels of adventure, which
he hopes soon to place in the publishers’ hands.
Notwithstanding the current vogue he still
believes in the homely naturalness of incident and
treatment.
At Burribooroona (N.S.W.), where the
local member is the Be-all and End-all, a friend
of his from Sydney, the other day, met in the
(one) street the great man’s father, whom he
accosted, holding out his hand with—“We have
met before. You are Mr. Bullethead.” “No,”
replied the old gentleman, seemingly oblivious to
the pathos of the situation, “ I am only Mr.
Bullethead’s father.”

Ex-Chief Justice Yilleneuve Smith, Tas.,
was a most comfortable-looking official on the
bench. Nothing seemed to impair his serenity.
Some years ago he sentenced a 72-year-old burglar
to 14 years in Launceston; whereupon the old
sinner exclaimed, “ Well, I’ll see you out, you
old blank, anyhow !” Yilleneuve smiled sweetly
as the prisoner was removed. He bore no malice.
“ A real good fellow ! I’d do almost any-
thing for him,” exclaimed a member of the Vic.
Cabinet on being asked by a confrere whether he
knew a certain out-of-luck ex-railway employe.
“ Glad of that,” returned the latter, grimly, as he
produced a subscription-list. “ What can I put
you down for, old man ? ” “ Huar-ah ! You can
book me for half-a-crown. Good-bye, I’m in a
hurry.”
The sire of a certain well-represented Tas-
manian family was chiefly noted for his remark-
able digestive powers. He was accustomed to
visit the larder between meals, and to convey
secretly therefrom to his person the contents of
various dishes. One day a laborer rushed up to
the farm-house and frightened the family into fits
with the news that the boss had been thrown from
his horse and had his brains knocked out. The
entire household fled wildly to the scene of the
catastrophe, and found the old man sitting in the
middle of the road with a sickly smile on his face,
and the remains of a black-currant pudding trick-
ling slowly from his hair. He had planted the
delicacy in his hat. Quite a number of members
of other old Tas. families in Parliament are re-
ported to be puddin’-headed.

A former Governor of New South Wales,
with a reputation for meanness, once ventured to
question the price of a pair of boots made by his
shoemaker, a Scotchman, and a well-known
identity of those days. “Well,” responded
Sandy, “ I mak the boots, and I mak the prices,
and if ye dinna like the price ye needna tak the
boots.” At Mark Foy’s neither boots nor price
are ever called into question. The most famous
factories in Europe and America “mak” his
boots, and Mark Foy himself “maks” the price.*

The death is an-
nounced in Loudon
from influenza of
Charles Day, once a
proprietor of S. A. Re-
gister, who was just
about to flee back to

Gov. Brassey’s aide-de-camp caused quite
a flutter in Vic. Cabinet circles, a day before His
Ex. arrived, by solemnly announcing that Mr.
and Mrs. B. would be much annoyed if their name
was pronounced Brass-ay. “ The correct pronun-
ciation is 'Bra- cey ’ —his lordship is really most
particular about it—not Brass-ay on any account,
tf you please.” So the whole Cabinet took to pro-
nouncing “ Bra-cay ” in front of looking-glasses,
and woke their wives up in the night by saying
“ Bra- cey ”in their sleep. The ex-chairman of
the Railway Board—Judge Casey—is childishly
delighted at the discovery that he is the only truly
great man in Melbourne whose name rhymes with
that of His Ex.

Nathan Rothschild was talking to Ade-
laide’s new Governor, Fowell Buxton, the other
day. Said the banker : “ Sometimes, to amuse
myself, I give a beggar a sovereign. He thinks
it’s a mistake, and, for fear I should find him out,
runs as hard as he can. I advise you to give
a beggar a sovereign sometimes. It is very
amusing. ”
Another N.S.W. Supreme Court Judge is
expected to retire on pension within the next six
months. Assuming the present Government to be
in power then, a favorable opportunity will occur
for trying the effect of a promotion from the
District Court bench. Mr. Want could, but will
not, assert a political claim, and there is no other
first-class barrister-politician at the back of the
Ministry. Hence an opening is made for Judge
Murray, the District Court senior, and the pro-
motion would have public approval.
Sculptor Simonetti left Sydney for Italy
by the German mail-boat to superintend the oast-
ing in bronze of the last figures modelled for the
Governor Phillip statue. This commemoration
in metal of the commonplace naval captain who
was Botany Bay’s first governor and gaoler, is a
legacy from the reign of Parkes, and will cost the
bleeding country something like .£12,000. One of
the “reproductive works” in which J. Bull’s
money has been invested.
