Waiter Buller is mentioned among the
probable successors to Westby Perceval in the
M. Agent-Generalship, but the betting is in
favor of Buckley, the Turveydrop of the M.L.
Ministry. Buller is not a “persona grata” with
the “Kensington gang,’’having mortally offended
Somers Vine, a toady of H. R.H. Pat Buckley
was a struggling lawyer, until he married into
the influential Fitzherbert family, and in his
younger days was a thrifle “fresh,” but for some
years past he has settled down as a staid pater-
familias and a highly-dignified legislator.
Rev. Mother Real, of Monte St. Angelo,
North Sydney, who was successful defendant in
the habeas corpus case decided by N.S.W. Chief
Justice Darley last week—a case in which the
mother and paternal grandmother of an illegiti-
mate child sought to withdraw it from the
custody of the nuns —is a sister of Justice
Pat. Real, of Queensland. The latter’s por-
trait, with all the paraphernalia of office about
him, ornaments the convent parlor, and untutored
visitors occasionally offer up a prayer to the
picture, thinking it represents some canonised
Pope of the Middle Ages.
Naturalist M‘Cooey (whose name appears
to be Scotch-aboriginal) has discovered a new
lizard in the Dubbo district, and dedicated it to
N. Premier Reid, “in recognition of his
democratic principles.” Another explanation is
that the varmint has been dedicated to Mr. Reid
as a tribute to the wrigglesome flexibility of his
backbone. But The Bulletin is puzzled at that
lizard not having been named after Just loe
Minister Gould, who, in his official capacity, is
perhaps the most cold-blooded of a cold-blooded
series of Ministers. Perhaps M’Cooey is waiting
to find a new frog?
Hori Grey writes from London to his
friend Laishley, of Auckland: “ I am getting
more feeble every day as my age increases. The
climate here agrees very well with me. I may say
it is that in which I was brought up as a child.”
Grey’s worst complaint is extreme old age. In the
dog-days his condition was extremely critical, but
with that marvellous elasticity of his he rallied
sufficiently to make a trip to Sandhurst in com-
pany with Lord Stamford, his nephew, and to
visit the Military College where he was educated.
Mr. and Mrs. Seymour George, who passed
through Sydney a few days ago, en route to Eng-
land, will accompany the old man on his return to
Maoriland.
When octogenarian Parkes got married
again, the other day, for the third time, “he
did not kiss his 23-year-old bride, but inclined his
head to receive his first kiss from the third Lady
Parkes. The unexpressed invitation was accepted
by the bride, and she bestowed on her husband a
dutiful but half-timid salute. The marriage cere-
mony occupied nearly half-an hour, and through-
out the old man appeared to be greatly affected,
and at times it was with great apparent difficulty
that he could make the responses. The bride
had her own prayer-book, which she had carried
in hand all the morning, and as the ceremony
went on little pauses were caused as the ex-Premier
turned over the leaves of his book, since it ap-
peared that he had great difficulty in following
the service, so affected did he become. When the
solemn vows were taken Sir Henry remained on
his knees during the remainder of the ceremony
instead of standing, as is customary. The service
apparently had a great effect upon him, and the
veteran, who appeared in such good spirits at
first, appeared to be broken down completely.”
The curse of Dean again. Everybody is breaking
down.
J. B. Thurston, High Commissioner of the
Western Pacific, says the Fiji Times, was once
assistant in a small grocery in Mudgee; subse-
quently owned the store, but failed; went on a
trading cruise in the Pacific; got wrecked, but, as
one of a boat’s crew, reached laud safely, and
settled; became British Consul, then Fiji’s first
Governor, and was eventually appointed to the
post he now holds. . . . Which, says Thurston,
is all a lie. He never was a storekeeper in his life.

Madame Antonio, the startling gymnaste,
a portrait of whom was printed last week, has
been a trapeze-artist for 18 years— i.e., since she
was four years old. Her parents were both
trapezists, and her grandparents on both sides
were trapezists also. So that the law of heredity
is considerably responsible for Madame having
man’s muscles and being stronger, comparatively,
in the arms than in the body or legs. She is a
Roumanian, but was brought up in Paris almost
from infancy. She has exhibited in every large
European and American city, and in India and
China. Her biggest audience numbered 35,000, at
the Tivoli, Copenhagen, in ’B7. She can speak
French, English, German, Spanish and Italian.
She finds the Frenchman the most enthusiastic
spectator, the Englishman the slowest. She lives
most temperately, eschews potatoes, and drinks
only claret, lemonade, tea and water. Her meals
are breakfast, lunch, and supper after the show.
Her morning rehearsal is a half-hour on the
trapeze. In the afternoon, she walks or drives.
Her first accident was 10 years ago ; the trapeze
broke, letting her down 15ft. upon the floor, and
breaking a rib. Twelve months later, in another
such accident, she broke her ankle. Soon after
this she began practising her dive into the net,
and managed 25ft. Now, she will perch like a
bird 140 ft. above the net, take a full breath, coma
sailing headlong down, turning two somersaults
like a tumbler-pigeon, and falling upon her
back into the net. The impact knocks
the breath out of her, and the re-
bound flings her up yards; but she lands
back on her feet, and comes off smiling. Her
great feat causes her neither pain nor stiffness,
but comparative idleness during the long voyage
to Australia made her back tender, so that the
smack of the concussion gave her a tingling at
first. She uses no liniments, but resins her hands
to ensure her hold. She says she loves her work—
“it is everything to me. tip there, I never lose
my head—why, if I did”—and she laughs, “I
might fall upon my feet, break my knees, cause
concussion of the brain—and, if I fell upon my
face or breast, I should hurt myself badly.’’

Howard and William Goodrich (writer knows Mr. G.)
have discovered at Wilgress, Lake Carey District, an out-
crop of rich quartz running 60yds. with gold showing in
all parts. Beef 3ft. wide but opening out wider. The
stone was brought into Ooolgardie and is regarded as the
best battery quartz yet found in that district. An assay
of a bulk sample gave 640 z. to the ton." It is plea-
sant to hear the reef is widening, for at 640 z. a 3ft.
reef ought to pay decently,

the much-vaunted Corrinna was something like scz. from
about 8000 yards of stuff. This latter has let its property
on tribute.

Mr. Nathan Herman, the proprietor of the
Misfit Parlor in Wynyard-square, Sydney, will he
remembered in that city for many years as the
man who struck a new idea. In every country
where man is sufficiently civilised to have his
clothes and boots made to order, there is neces-
sarily a large accumulation of garments which
have been made to fit the frame of particular pur-
chasers, who didn’t call for them, or couldn’t pay
for them, or died, or absconded, or went
mad,, or got into _gaol before delivery, and
so left them on the maker’s hands. And
in every case, there is someone else about
whom the rejected article would fit, for
everybody’s clothes—except those of Dick Dead-
eye and the Hunchback of Notre Dame—fib
somebody else, and this person would be glad to
■ get a first-class garment at a reduced price, and
the builder of it would be pleased to get rid of it
at almost any sacrifice. The difficulty always has
been to bring the misfit and the man it will fib
together, and Mr. Herman solved the problem by
accumulating in one place misfits from all
the ends of the earth, so that everybody who
can possibly be fitted by human ingenuity
may go there and be satisfied. He is
also a universal provider—a dealer in safes,
crockery, furniture, bedding, carpets, linoleums,
oil cloths, drapery, jewellery, watches, cigars,
tobacco, tea (401 b chest for 30s. or 121 b. tin for
10s.), cutlery, fancy goods, ironmongery, &0., &c.
But the misifit idea was his own special inspira-
tion, and he is proud. of it. Even the famous
£2 bale, which contains a suit of clothes, two
shirts, two undershirts, six pairs socks, two ties,
two collars, six handkerchiefs, set studs, hat, pair
braces and pair of boots, all sent to the railway
station or wharf nearest to the-buyers’ residence
for 405., doesn't appeal to his soul like that great
original idea. Mr. Herman ha,s lately broken out
into pianos, firearms, ammunition, and other new
departments,' and makes gentlemen s suits for
.£1 9s. 6d., and supplies dress-suits at one-fourth
ttie usual price. Also, he has broken out into so
many other lines of business of late that no one
knows tor certain what he lias gpt, and the things
that lie hasn’t got could mostly be enumerated on
the back of one postage stamp, and even then the
stamp wouldn’t be unduly crowded.

Hospital Sunday at Bendigo was a wasted
opportunity for the marvellously-tailored Dan
Lazarus, M.L.A., to aid the cause of local charity.
Dan stood around the gates of the park whilst the
band was playing, and took up a collection in a
tin-box. He was gorgeously arrayed—tight-fitting
gloves, slim umbrella, everything new and glossy.
But scores of constituents, who would have been
glad to drop a modest contribution in a less-
appalling person’s box, shrank from the thought
of insulting this modern Lazarus with a bit of
silver, therefore his services represented a loss to
the fund. Next Hospital Sunday, if the authori-
ties are wise, they will enclose their senator in
a capacious tent and charge the masses sixpence
to go in and gaze upon his wearing-apparel.
Dr. Dowling raised a nice point at Rich-
mond (Melb.) Police Court, the other day, when
he declined to certify to the lunacy of a woman On
the grounds that her aberration was due to A
physical complaint curable at an ordinary hospital;
Magistrate Keogh waxed indignant and sent for
another physician, explaining that the so-
called lunatic-asylums are actually hospitals for
the insane. Nevertheless popular stupidity
attaches a stigma to inmates of them only second
to that attachable to occupants of gaols. Any-
how, Dr. Dowling’s delicacy was nothing to rave
about.
An editor whose office is on the line taken
by Gov. Brassey to the Exhibition on his arrival,
arranged to hang out from his window a calico
sign representing a woman hanging from a gibbet,
and with the inscription “The Executive
Chokes a Pregnant Woman Next Monday
Week ! ” But some ladies who have a finger in
the editor’s literary pie got wind of the proposal,
and promptly squelched it, on the grounds that it
would discredit the organ at Guv’rnent ’Ouse. 8°
the banner was finally handed over to Messrs.
Fleming, White and Co. for Sunday use on Yarra
Bank. A great opportunity lost.
Another specimen of the work the Aus-
tralian cable - combination performs. S. M.
Herald on Oct. 24 :
London, Oct. 22.— 1 n the French Chamber cf Deputies
to-day M. Henri Briason eulogised the heroism of the
French troops in Madagascar. In answer to interpella-
tions, it was announced that cablegrams had been
received stating that a disastrous explosion had taken
place on board the transport King, by which 17 of those
on board, mostly officers, were killed on the spot. The
others, it was added, rushed the boats, with the result
that 500 men were drowned. Two hundred others clung
to the wreck for 17 hours before they were rescued.
Sydney Daily Telegraph version of the same
cable: —
Explosion on a Chinese transport. The soldiers rush
the boats. Five hundred drowned. Two hundred res-
cued after 17 hours. London, Tuesday night.—Details
have been received with respect to the terrible explosion
which took place on board a Chinese transport at Kin-
chow, a seaport at the head of the Gulf of Liao-tong.
Most of the officers on board the ship were killed by the
explosion, which completely wrecked the vessel. The
soldiers rushed the boats, with the result that 500 were
drowned. 200 others clung to the wreck for 17 hours
before they were rescued.

The proprietors of that well-known
remedy, ' end’s Extract, issue a pamphlet con-
taining reprints of a number of testimonials from
medical men, all of whom speak of it in very
high terms. The .extract is a preparation of
Hamawetis, which is a valuable curative agent for
ulcers, nasal catarrh, varicose veins, hemorrhage,
dysentery, and many other diseases. It is also a
powerful antiseptic, and applied externally to
injuries or inflamed parts it is of great assistance
for the relief of pain. Pond’s Extract is one of
the best preparations of this drug in the market,
and has stood the test of many years’ service.

A BRIDAL SONG.
Photo, by J. H. Newman.
THE THIRD LADY PARKES.
Nimble Cupid capers madly
Round the bridegroom and the bride;
Hymen greets them, smiling gladly
On love’s passion sanctified.
Let the clang of joy-bells weighty
.Echo far o’er land sea—
Lo ! the grand old man of eighty
Weds a girl of twenty-three!
He the oak and she the ivy
Clinging to his manly form,
Not dead yet, but all alivey—
Full of youthful vigor warm.
Here’s a toast; come, fill your rummers !
May they long united be—
Parkes, the swain of eighty summers,
And his wife of twenty-three.

Dear Bulletin, —This is not Southland
(M.L.) Acclimatisation Society’s first move
towards acclimatising the opossum. Six or seven
years ago ’possums were placed at Orepuke and
Colao Bay, and those localities are literally alive
with them. The cold climate, too, has improved
their skin—the fur is much thicker and better
than that of the Australian animal. — ScuiP.
The success of the Winfcon (Q.) bore is a
remarkable example of perseverance rewarded.
The contract was first taken in ’B9. The contract
depth of 2500 ft. was attained in ’93. As no
aitesian water was struck, the Govt, authorised
another 1000 ft. In Oct., ’94, a depth of 3810 ft.
was attained, and 100,000 gals. day. The Govt,
authorised anotlnr 500 ft., and at a depth of
3555 ft. a fresh supply was struck. On June 22 of
this year the steam-whistle of the engine roused
the Wintonites, who turned out and found them-
selves the proud possessors of the deepest bore in
Queensland (4010 ft.), and million gals, of water
per diem. Six years’ hard work was thus de-
s rvedly rewarded. The contractor remarks, by
the way, that he always knows when he is
approaching artesian water by the strong mineral
smell issuing from the bore.
When the British Board of Trade author-
ised Mr. Stewart, the Senior Official Receiver, to
hold an enquiry into the affairs of the N.Z.
L. and M. Agency, it was heard before Mr.
Justice Vaughan Williams, reputedly a very able
Judge, whose forte lies in disentangling intricate
commercial transactions. The evidence of the
directors, officers, and valuers turned mainly on
the value of certain properties. There had
been two separate valuations—one made dur-
ing prolonged drought, when the runs looked
like well-trodden stubble, and the gaunt cattle
were propping themselves up against the trees;
the second, dated a few months later, after rain
and warm sunshine, when the grass had freshened
up and the stock greatly improved. Of course,
the second valuation was much higher than the
first. It was this that excited the lofty ridicule
of the Judge. With raised eyebrows and a sar-
donic smile, he asked the witness, Mr. “Tom”
Russell, whether he thought English people were
so easily gulled as to be induced to believe that a
few showers of rain could make a difference of
several thousands of pounds in the value of a run.
The bar and the public grinned in sympathy with
the sapient Judge. None of them had the slight-
est conception of the effects of a drought. Their
notions of an Australian run were drawn from an
English meadow, or Regent’s Park.

Vic. Supreme Court decides that the
transference of railway-tickets bought in another
province is not illegal. For instance, a ticket
from Adelaide to Melbourne and return may be
sold in Melbourne, and the Vie. Commissioners
have no power to interfere, the law applying only
to tickets sold within the province. The wonder
is that anybody; from anywhere with a return-
ticket in Melbourne these times should want to
dispose of it.

A Dream of Dry Bones .
[FOR THE BULETTIN.]
When I read that some surveyors had found a
skeleton on the edge of the desert in the Never
Never, I felt guilty, because scratched on the
billy-lid beside the whitened bones was the name
of my old schoolmate. He had died of thirst in
the desert. Most likely he had wandered away
from some shanty “in the horrors.” Anyhow
they found his bones when the dingoes had gnawed
them and the ants had eaten away all signs of
flesh. Perhaps the ravenous kites had picked out
his eyes before the flesh was cold. Close by was
an old, rusty billy, and near it the skeleton of a
dog. Nothing remained to tell who the man had
been except the name on the billy. Evidently his
had been a terrible end, and my guiltiness was
because I could have saved him from such a death.
Once, years ago, he and I slept in the skilhon
room of my father’s old bark-roofed house. At
midnight the moon shone through the holes in the
roof. The moonbeams played on the old wooden
posts of our bedstead and on the ’possum-rug that
was our only blanket. They showed me the white
face and dark, curly hair of the boy about my
own age—who slept beside me. How I envied
him ! My hair was red, and my freckled face was
sunburnt and blistered, audit smarted so that for
hours I lay awake, counting the sides of bacon
and hams, and bags of pumpkin-seed and dried
peaches that hung from the rafters above my
head. The mosquitoes buzzed and the dingoes
howled on the moonlit hill behind the house, and
the morepork hooted and crooned on the edge of
the forest. By and by a ’possum came frisking
along the roof. I watched his antics for a while
drowsily, and then dropped off to sleep.
In my dream I saw a wide, barren stretch of
sand fringed with stunted bush, and bordered with
ragged belts of scrub. Here and there grew mis-
shapen trees, bent and gnarled ; then mile on mile
nothing but scrub and sand, where the whitened
bones of men and animals that had perished were
strewn along the grassless plains, and a dead
silence was over all. Overhead a cloudless sky
gleamed and dazzled down to the horizon s
edge, and the sun seemed on fire, and the
sands of the desert burned. In the shade
of a clump of trees the kites gathered round
a man with weary, swollen feet. His bps
were parched and blackened, and he had glaring
eyes. His dog, sore-footed too, lay at his feet.
The inan rose when the kites came near
cursed them and pelted them, and they flew off
clamoring and quavering, but they came back and
taunted him, and he cursed at them till his lips
cracked and bled. Then he moaned and muttered
to himself, and called to his dog, and staggered
away from the demons sent to haunt him.
All day he walked, and there was no track, and
his throat was parched, and a great thirst tortured
him, and all the pitiless day he was haunted by
the memories of his life. His feet bled, and the
merciless, mighty sun poured down upon him ;
but he was mad, and saw green fields and cool
rivers and shining lakes that fled away and
mocked him ; and only the fierce, bright sun and
the burning sand of the desert remained. All day
long his head was filled with memories of the old
farm and the canefields. The shearing-sheds and
the shanties—the gay city life and the gambling
hells. The dance-room and the old school-house,
and the cool solitude of the prison cell. All the
jumbled memories of his past life seethed in his
maddened brain as he staggered on—outward to
the desert; and overhead, as lie walked, the kites
circled, and the hot winds blew, and the glaring
sun blazed redder and set; and the sands grew
cool, and again he lay beneath a tree, but the dog
sat out on the edge of the shade and whined, for
the man’s eyes were bloodshot and he foamed at
the mouth. The sun went down, and the kites
gathered near and whistled and chattered among
themselves, for it was in the drought, and they
were fat. The red moon rose a perfect ball of
fire. Then the dingoes came, and the dog chased
them away, and they howled and waited for the
man to die—and he raved and dug in the sand,
and when the grim, grey, dewless dawn came he
was dead. Then the dog licked his face and
crawled to his feet and died too. Then came the
kites and the crows, and they fought and had a
great feast, and the hot winds blew again, and the
sun shone, and the sky gleamed, and the sands of
the desert burned.
I awoke. The ’possum still frisked on the roof,
and the boy still slept. The moonbeams shone on
the old gun hanging on the wall. I shivered at
the horror of my dream. Better that the boy
should die in his sleep than live for years and
perish in the desert. I thought to reach for the
gun to kill him. I who had envied him would
kill him—in mercy and in pity I would kill him.
But the moonbeams faded and the moon set, and
the cold, grey dawn came over the hill. The boy
woke and stretched himself and yawned, and
called me “ Ginger,” and told me to get up. The
calves in their pens close by bellowed, and the
cows answered them from the paddocks. It was
milking-time, and the world began to be astir.
But to-day when I read the name which in wild
delirium he had scratched on the billy-lid, I felt
guilty, because I knew that I could have saved
him from the death he died.
Q.

The Bulletin’s History of the Dean Case
mailed to any address in New South Wales for
Gd ; Australasia, ; foreign, 7d. in stamps.
The generation has almost passed away
that used to daily interrogate each other in the
words, “ Who struck Buckley?” The person in
question was “Wild Buckley,” well-known in the
early days of Victoria, and his bad luck was so
phenomenal that the phrase “Buckley’s chance”
came to signify about the worst kind of fortune
that could befall a man. Buckley it was who,
when rescued from the blacks, could only recollect
the one English word, “ bread.” Very so. n after
going into exile among the natives, his moleskin
pants succumbed, and he had to take to kangaroo-
skins. But, in these days, bushmen have a far
better chance than Buckley, as Mark Foy’s great
stores of clothing are within a few days’ reach of
the remotest of them.*

Analysis of Suicide.
Who among us does not feel pity, if not horror
and contempt, for those who have so lost all re-
gard for law, human and divine, as to premedita-
tingly take measures for self-destruction?
Want of self-reliance; lack of self-mastery ; an-
tipathy to existence, and a longing to escape life’s
responsibilities are the frequent excuses offered by
those who in “ fits of temporary insanity ” rudely
out the thread of life.
While there may be some excuse for these, there
is none for so-called criminal carelessness; for
those who cultivate a disregard for kindly advice,
and under-estimate admonition. When lifeseems
a burden, when the nights are long and sleepless,
when the continual strain threatens life, many
recklessly turn a deaf ear and cold shoulder to a
friend’s proffered advice. Why should prejudice
and arrogance so often prevent one following the
dictates of conscience? “Fortunately,” says Mr.
Hugh Barnett, retired Government Officer, Roraai
Queensland, “I accepted the advice of a friend
and took Warner’s Safe Cure. For a long time I
endured terrible suffer ing from liver disease. The
pains under the shoulder-blades, down the back,
and in my chest and sides were so severe that I
could get no rest day or night. My appetite
failed, and I became so weak and worn out that I
was obliged to give up work of all kinds. After
spending much time and money in other direc-
tions, I commenced taking Warner’s Safe Cure,
and after using several bottles I am thankful tb
say that I have quite recovered my good health,
and consequently have no hesitation in advising
those suffering from liver disease to try this medi-
cine, in preference to all other remedies that may
be offered to them.”
SPORTING NOTIONS.
This year’s Melbourne Cup is quite likely to be
a favorites’ race as in 1889, when Ihe winner started at
eights, the second horse at sevens, and the third at fours
to one. lint there are no wai m favorites thus far. After
the Melbourne Slakes and Derby have been decided
punters will “know something," for each of the races
aforesaid must inevitably reveal one or more “ good
things,” uni ss the condilion of Fleminglon course on
Saturday is abnormal. A muddy gallop is about the
worst of all guides to fair-weather "foim." The three-
year-olds, this year, appear exceptionally good in com-
parison with their elders, and an unusual amount of Cup
interest will attach to the Derby struggle if no particular
breakdowns occur meanwhile. This is such a dry season
that the rush of “breakdowns” may beat the Dean-case
record, nevertheless one is bound to leave accid- nts out
of the question in discussing Cup chances. Given a
vision of the Derby and a photo, of the Stakes
finish, the writer could almost be persuaded to back
his own tip for the Cup. Anraria will seem a sound
selection for Tuesday’s handicap if she should heat Water-
fall, Wallace, Rewi, and Usculator in the Derby. The
writer fancies Aurar'a for the classic event in preference
to the colts, all of which have pretensions to win the Cup.
Fifteen of the last 25 Cup winners have been
“ seasoned ” animals—four years old, nr upwards—carry-
ing middle weights, from 7st. 31b. to Bst. 71b. Therefore
a prophet must show some reasonable excuse for expect-
ing either a top-weight, or a young horse, to win t his hard-
run race. On the face of precedent one couldn’t do
better than put the names of all the 7st. 31b. to Bst. 71b.
horses in a bat, pick one of them at random, and
back it. As regards top-weights down to 8s:. 81b.—
the probable starters are Brockleigh, The Admiral, Hop-
scotch, Devon, Delaware, Quiver, lioia, Elswick and
Trenchant. We have bad evidence, at one time or other,
tiiat Brockleigh, Hopscotch and Hova cannot live light
through a fast two-mile race, Devon and Quiver, it is
clear, can stay well enough, and the writer assumes that
they are likely lo beat, Delaware, The Admiral and Tren-
chant, yei neither Devon nor Quiver is a very attractive
chance with Bst. 121 h. or Bst. 13lh. to carry.
Very few of the old ones, classed as rniddle-
w'eights, have ever done anything to make them appear
dangerous candidates. This is a poor reason
for despising their chances, seeing that Tarcoola
and Glenlot h were open lo the same objection
before their victories came off. The writer will take I
Preston, The Trier, Chesteiman, and Taranaki to he pos- '
sihle winners, but less likely than Dreamland, Music, or
Monastery. Two of these three are specially difficult;
ohaiicts to gauge. Dreamland promised to he a brilliant
horse until he fell ill, fifteen months ago, and now
nobody knows how much, or little, that illness has dis-
comfited Dreamland’s fulure prospects. He ran pretty
well in tiie Caulfield Slakes, and lias a very fine outline !
anyhow. Music, the “dark” Tasmanian champion, may
lie a real good one. Monastery, from S.A., lias a fair
ciiai oe for the Cup on some of her running, and no chance
at all on other running. One would pick this st>lish
mare from a crowd as a likely winner, and the writer
reckons Monastery capable of worrying Devon and Quiver j
with a 141 h. advantage in the handicap. Of the matured
lightweights only Lord Richmond seems worth consider-
ing, though Palo Alto may find backers. Lord Richmond
is a fine, improving horse tiiat ought to make a hold show
forthe Cup, and owner Jellett fancies him immensely.
But Jellelt’s opinions about his own cattle are seldom
justified.
The writer, then, can’t see much to choose
between Monastery, Quiver, Devon, Dreamland, and Loid
Richmond, nor any reason why a first-class three-year-old
shouldn’t heat the lot. None of the crack “classic ” per-
formers of 1894, or 1893, are left in this year’s Cup crowd.
Tiie surviving veterans don’t look templing invist-
menls for the great two-mile event, hence Delaware’s
position as a nominal first favorite these last few
weeks. A little money would have brought any-
thing else to the fore. They seem to be at the
mercy of any brilliant youngsters, which brings us hack
to the question whether this season’s three-vear-olds are
above tiie average. Probably. Waterfall won the Caul-
field Cup in excellent lime and style. Te VVliiti ran pro-
minent ly in the same race after being badly beaten for
the Guineas by Wallace. Rewi, again, is said to pull over
Te Whiti on the training-track, and has been backed for
the Cup on his private form and good looks. Osoulator
a half-brother to The Bulletin's old friend, Tranter, is
an imposing thoroughbred, almost certain to make a
name for himself if he remains sound. As for Anraria
she comes from S. Australia with tiie reputation of being
a “ smasher,” and appearances favor the impression tiiat
the Ally's friends are not overrating her. Asa two-year-old
Auraria (in receipt of 2st.) beat The Possible over 8, 9 and
10 furlongs, at a time when Mr. Gollan’s mare wasshowing
good form. The handioapper gave The Possible Bst. 31h.
for the Cup, and Auraria, as a three-year-old, will have to
carry no more than weight-for age if she wins the Derhv.
After her defeat for the Maiibyrnong Plate the South
Australian flyer ran six times as a two-year-old—winning
four races outright, making a dead-beat for first place in
another, and finishing second on the other occasion. This
season, Auraria was asked to win the S.A. Derby, when
not nearly fit, and she cantered away with the race. A
consistently fiist-class performer, and a beautiful filly
Auraria will be worth hacking for the Derby in prtfer-
ence to Wallace, or even Waterfall (who probably s
better than tiie Victorian pet). At time of writing she
is not doing much on the Flemington track, and the
books offer 5 to 1 on tiie principle that a filly is likely to
“go off” at any minute in the early summer. All the
same Auraria is The Bulletin's tip fo'r the Derby, and its
tip for the Cup is the Derby winner. Also Tub
Bulletin ventures to predict that the three first horses in
tiie Cup race will come from a lot of seven—viz., tiie six
placed for either Melbourne Stakes or Derby, with Music
thrown in as a likely outsider.
The Officer, though penalised 101 b.. has a really
good show to win the Maiibyrnong Plate, and perhaps
The Heroine will pick up a minor handicap during the
V.R.C. meeting.

• • • •
Zimmerman, allegedly suffering from sandy-
bligl.t, diarrhoea, insomnia, and other things, was enabled
by his indomitable pluck to down Parsons in the Half-
Mile at Adelaide, and run second to Parsons in the Five
Miles with only a length and a half b( tween them. Zim.’s
half-mile was done in Imin, 6 1-5-ec.— a new record for
Austraia; still, considering that Zim. and Paisons have
each cone a quarter in 27isec., this time is nothing to
bellow about, especially as Hie first lap was paced. Zim.
scratched himself for the handicap races (he will ride in
no handicap races in Australia), whereat the insatiable
herd butted their bullock heads against the palings and
hissed and groaned.
In the Five Miles, Parsons hung on to the
tandem and made the pace a scorcher—still Zim. kept
there. Zim.’s condition didn’t permit of his picking his
machine up as of yore and shooting along the last
hundred yards with the whiz of a bullet. Probably
Parsons will ride with Zim. right through the Australian
tour.
♦ * * *
A London paper says that bicyclist Zimmer-
man’s great success is due to hisahnormally large heart,
whicli is declared by doctors to he much bigger than
the average. Strange that no mention is made of another
not unimportant feature in Zim’s success,— viz., his large
and fan-like ears. When Zim was racing in Adelaide, the
writer was particularly struck with this peculiarity, and
calculates that the American is at least 2secs. to the mile
quicker, owing solely to the ingenious way he assists
his machine liy woiking his ears windmill-fashion.

Zimmerman tells Melb. Argus that the entry
of his name in the Austral Wheel Race was not authoiised,
and that he will not, owing to other engagements, be able
to compete. Truth is that Zim yearned to ride, hut nob
for the pure love of sport. Hard cash is his basis. He
wanted 1100 paid down to ride in the Austral, besides
the prospect of winning the big prize. Melb. B.C. very
rightly didn’t see the force of pay ing any competitor to
ride in a lace which is a pure sport affair. So Zim has
“ other engagements.”

The latest tricycle is semi-automatic. The shaft
carrying the hind wheels has a petroleum-motor attached.
On starting, a few pedal strokes only are needed, ami
pedals are then tin own out of gear, a touch on a button
causes an electric spark to ignite the petroleum-motor

ti./liter requires to lie a persistently self-advertising blow-
fly rather than a game fighter.—Tuoeold W.

Paper teeth are “ made in Germany.” A
set in use for 13 years is still going strong.
The other day, at Sandridge (Vie.), a
sailor prosecuted the mate of his vessel for assault,
and exit ibited damages caused by blows from an iron
bar. The Bench (which included Fat Men interested
in shipping) said that there was no doubt of the
mate s guilt, but the presiding Solon pointed out
that the A. 8., in refusing to perform the task set
him, was guilty of as serious an offence, and the
case was dismissed. Which shows that, in the
opinion of the paunchy J.P., the lash has simply
given place to the crowbar as a means of implant-
ing humility and discipline in the breast of the
| common sailor. Another reason—the ten-thou-
sandth—why the Vie. honorary bench should be
sponged off the slate.
At Wellington (M.L.), where the China-
men have such a hold, an Irishman poured forth
a lament to the P.M. : “ ’Tisn’t because I can’t
pay me rint, but it’s the throuble and disgrace of
paying up to a Cliej uamiu. For years I paid up
to a whoite-mahu, and now I’Ve got to pay a Chey-
namin. A cratur that came wid nothing at all
wanst, and now has got all the land round about
and me own that I used to pay up to a whoite-
mm, and now I have to pay me rint to a (Jhey-
nannu. And I can, and you can, do nothing at
all, ses ye, and 1 must go on payin' me nut to a
Cheynamin ; or lor, on’y to think of it, to go on
payiu and payin’ to a Chey ”
Dear Bulletin, —You take a one-eyed
view of the stamp-collector’s grievance. “F.M.G.
Cook is reprinting old postage-scamps for the
good of the N.S.W. revenue, and apparently
intends to continue at the work. Consequently
the British person named Evans will have to
simmer down. N.S.W. can go on issuing its own
stamps as long as it pleases.” There’s no doubt
that N.S.W. can issue its own stamps, and the
British person will have to simmer down ; never-
theless P.xVl. G. Cook is debasing the philatelic
currency, as Evans complains, and enriching the
revenue by illegitimate means. A value for out-
of-issue postage-stamps was created wholly and
solely by collectors, who have paid handsome
prices for “rare specimens,” and such speci-
mens became rare for the reason that they
had been officially made obsolete by the
various postal departments. Mr. Cook to-day
is trading in what are (so far as collections
go) practically forgeries of old-time stamps, the
rarity of which is owing to the former action of
his department in changing the issue. He
doesn’t reprint them as postage-stamps, but as
copies of stamps which have derived a fancy
value from the fact that they ceased long ago to pass
through the post. Furthermore, the department
is charged with misrepresenting the age of these
repiint stamps by means of old postmarks. Any
number of petty state postal departments have
condescended to issue reprints to dealers at a
paltry profit, or perhaps the men who
had the old plates under their control
struck off copies for their own small
private beneht. But a plain reprint of a stamp is
quite a different matter to a reprint that professes
to have been defaced by the Government as a
stamp going through the post 50 years ago, inas-
much as a postmark is an official guarantee of age
for a stamp. In Germany, the other day, a man
got a stiff sentence of imprisonment for putting
false postmarks on reprint stamps, though his
swindle, of course, injured nobody except the
philatelists, whosaem to be recognised as property-
owners in that well-educated country. Mr. Cook’s
way of doing good for the N.S.W. revenue is un-
doubtedly crooked.— Sydney Yuse.

Even the aboriginal waxes a-weary of the
Boodlewraith. Mr. A. Petrie, a well-known
Bananalander, the other day met a blackfellow,
an ex-tracker, who spoke excellent English. The
nig. had a grievance—he had received no blanket
at the annual distribution. A long tirade on the
matter concluded with : “ Well, if I don’t get a
blanket next time Government ’ll hear about it.
I’ll join the Labor-party ! ”

Herr Rassmussen. August, 1894.
Dear Sir,—ln the year of 1871 I was eight days in the water sheej -
washing, and contracted rheumatism and stiffness in the joints, and
have tried all advertised medicines. When I came to Sydney I had a
great difficulty in walking, and went to the Turkish baths, which did
no good. Passing your window I saw a testimonial from Mr. E. W.
O’Sullivan, M.P., with the same complaint as I had, which led me to

T
HE
COLONIAL
BANK
OF
AUSTRALASIA
LIMITED,
THE FIFTH REPORT OF THE DIRECTORS
—OF —
THE COLONIAL BANK OF AUSTRALASIA LIMITED,
To be presented to the Shareholders at the Fifth Ordinary General Meeting to be held at the Bank,
126 Elizabeth Street, at noon on
TUESDAY, OCTOBER 29, 1895.
R, IB IP O R T.
The Directors beg to submit to the Shareholders their Fifth Report, with a Balance-sheet and
Statement of Profit and Loss for the half-year ended the 30th September, duly audited.
After providing for expenses of management, interest accrued on deposits, rebate on bills current,
and tax on note circulation, the net profit, including the balance brought forward from previous half-
year amounted to JHO23 Os. 10d., which the directors recommend be carried forward to next half-year.
Since the last half-yearly meeting the Bank has arranged with the holders of its deferred fixed
deposit receipts for — . . ,
Ist. A substantial reduction in the rate of interest.
2nd. An ample extension of time for the repayment of its deferred deposits.
3rd. A provision for making all new business since 10th July, 1893, whether current account
or new fixed deposit receipts, preferential to the deferred deposit receipts.
The bank has had the benefit of the reduced rate of interest for only a portion of the half-year
under review, and the balance to be carried forward would have been larger but for the expensesuf the
amended scheme of compromise, the whole of which have been charged to the profits for the half-year.
The amendment of the Companies Act, referred to in the last report, authorising the reduction ot
capital by writing off losses, has not yet been passed, bub will probably be in force next year.
The fifth ordinary general meeting of shareholders will be held at the head office of the company
126 Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, on TUESDAY, the 29th day of OCTOBER, 1895, at noon.
By order of the Board.
3 SELBY PAXTON, General Manager.
Melbourne, 22nd October, 1895.
THE COLONIAL BANK OF AUSTRALASIA LIMITED.
BALANCE-SHEET, 30th SEPTEMBER, 1895.

Hanging in lawyer Crick’s office in Philhp-
street, Sydney, is the free pardon issued to Biittner
on the very morning he was to have been hanged
on a charge of criminally assaulting the woman
Lennox, whose real character Crick discovered at
the last moment. It is the only free pardon ever
issued in Australasia i to a prisoner lying under
capital sentence, and it was all that Biittner was
able to give his advocate.
There was once a hairdresser at Geelong
He had four sons. One of these is now Australian
editor of the Review of Reviews ; the second is
Dean of Ohristohurch (M.L.); and a third is in
the M.L. Legislature. They all agree that the
fourth brother, who died, was the cleverest of the
quartette. Matthew Davies and his brothers also
hail from Geelong.

The Freaks
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
True, he ain’t no account as a nag,
ah’ I’m not goin’ to boast of his blood {
If I liked I could pitch you a mag
’Bout his sire, once a prince of the stud ;
Give performances colored and plain,
An’ a pedigree long es me arm—
Which is style, but I’m straight in the main,
So he ain’t of the Wangdoodle strain,
Nor his dam wasn’t Kate or The Charm.
Kiddle-headed an’ spavined ! Well, p’raps.,
Yes ? his legs is all over the shop,
An’ his pacin’s described by the chaps
Es a sort of a wallaby hop.
He ain’t good over sticks, an’ a mile
In four-thirty’s his best up to date;
An’ he’s jest pure Gehenna for guile,
But I wouldn’t sell out for a pile,
Cos I’m not goin’ to dog on a mate.
See, I’m here, an’ he’s yonder, of course,
But I might a-been crow-bait by now—
Once my life seemed to hang on that horse,
An’ I didn’t get left. That is how !
They’ve bin tellin’ you—-Billy an’ Spence?
Ah, they’re mighty smart men down the creek,
An’ they won’t allow horses has sense,
But jest guy it es chance or pretence
When I tell what was done by The Freak.
But I’m here, an’ he’s there—that’s enough 1
We were out ’mong the Misery Hills.
’Course you don’t know the country. It’s rough ;
An’ the man that it corners it kills.
I can’t figure what happened us quite,
But we came in a heap, me an’ him.
When I knew who I was it was night,
An’ my head an’ my chest wasn’t right.
An’ the bone poked right outer this limb.
Fer a spell I felt horribly sick
While I held there a meetin’ of me,
Proposed—“ It is U P with Dick.”
Put, an’ carried unanermously.
Broken-legged, fifteen mile from the Creek —
I weighed chances, an’ gave up the case,
But I didn’t deal fair by The Freak,
Till he limped to me, staggered an’ weak,
An’ he flopped his ole lip in my face.
Do ? I fondled his nose like a fool,
An’ I called him love names without end ;
Though I ain’t a soft man as a rule,
There is times when I sorter unbend.
’Tain’t no use now to talk of the pain
I endoored es I struggled to climb
To his back frum a log, or explain
How I fell back again an’ again ;
But I gave up exhausted in time,
An’ I flung meself down on the ground,
An’ I cursed an’, yes, maybe I cried,
But The Freak he came nosin’ around,
An’ he rolled over right by my side.
Don’t you try to explain, I’m content
That he knew just es well es could be,
’Cos i looked in his eye es he bent,
By the Lord, an’ I saiv what he meant,
An’ that’s good enough talkin’ fer me.
Well, I crawled on his back es he lay,
An’ he heaved himself up again, so,
An’ then struck out fer home, an’ till day
I hung on him, but how I don’t know.
Not a thing do I mind after that
’Fore I came round all right at the whim,
Spread out on the bunk of Big Mat,
With a doc. on the job from The Flat,
An’ my leg fairly timbered and trim.
Yes, I’ve heard all the mag of the men—
That he wanted to roll or to die,
An’ it’s true that he’s kicked me since then,
An’ he’s likewise uncommonly sly ;
But I’m here. If they talk fer a week
That one fact isn’t goin’ to change,
An’ I owe it this day to The Freak
That a crow isn’t clippin’ his beak
On my rib-bones out back by the range.
EDWARD DYSON.

After Shearing
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Thev met out back, in one of those long, strag-
gling bush pubs, with its beer-stained and mos-
quito-oovered wooden partitions. Shearing was
over, the sheds out out; their work was just start-
ing. Jack hadn’t shorn at any of the sheds—he
never did; his shed was a pub, his shears clubs,
hearts, &0., and he was a good workman in his
own line. So was “Ginger.” I never heard his
other name ; they said he came from Queensland.
Jack was seated on a cask in the bar, shears in
pocket, waiting for a sheep —or, as he termed it, a
“dook.” Ginger swaggered in, he looked every
inch a “ dook ” —it paid him.
“ Two drinks, missis—’course you’ll join.”
“ Been shearin’? ”
“ Rather.”
“Melbourne, I suppose?”
“ Well, I’m d d.”
“ So’m I; we’ll start in mornin’.”
“ Same again, missus. By Gawd, it’s ’ot.”
Ginger proposed poker. They started. Each
feigned sleepiness, each ivas sleepy; more liquor,
higher stakes. It really was hot; each actually
fell asleep. . . . Ginger awoke, took in the posi-
tion of affairs and the stakes at the same time,
and had “faked ’’Jack’s hand when the latter
awoke.
“Hello, mate, where’s the boodle? ”
“ Oh, that’s all right. I had the best of the
deal that time, boss —look at yer ’and.”
Jack looked —and recognised a kindred spirit.
“ Yes, indeed yer did. Yer woke fust /”

Oscar Wilde’s family is living at Buenos
Ayres.
On Butterbone station, N.S.W., lately,
two hunters, in 14 weeks, shot over 7000 kangaroos
—an average of 500 a week. One period of 14
days resulted in 1700 !
ONLY FOUR TO MAN THE PUMPS.
Dear, dear! When you come to think of it how
closely related things are ; how one thing brings
up another. Ideas are like a lot of beads on a
string, aren’t they ?
A letter I have just been reading makes me re-
member what happened to me one winter about
twenty years ago. The story is too long to tell
here, so 111 merely give you the tail-end of it. I
was supercargo on a bark bound from London to
Rio. A tremendous gale, lasting five days,
wrecked us. Forty-eight hours after it ceased
there were four men and no more-left on the
vessel. The captain had been killed by a falling
spar, three of the crew washed overboard, and the
rest of the thip s company (save us four) went
away in the long-boat with the first and second
mates. We were taking in water through a leak
at the rate of six in hes an hour. Working with
all our might the four of us could pump that out
in forty minutes, but we must do it every hour.
It was awful wo k. For two days we kept it up,
without sleep Then we stopped, took to the
quarter boat and shoved off. The sea was quiet
—no wind. While we lay to within a mile of her
the ship threw up her nose and went down stern
first. We were picked up the next day by a
Danish b ig.
Now the odd thing is that the letter that re-
minded me of that experience has nothing what-
ever to say about ships. Please help me to find
out the association
The lady who w ites the letter says that in
July, 1881, she got a bad fright. Exac ly what it
was she doesn t tell. I v\it-h she did. Anyway it
so upsc her that she did it' get over the effects of
it for niue years. After that her appetite fell off;
she lost all real leli-h for food, and what she did
eat only made t ouble in-iead of nourishing her.
Jtgave her pain in the pit of the stomach and
(curiously enough) between ihe shoulders. She
says her eyes and skin p. esently turned yellow as
a but e cup. Her face and abdomen swelled, and
her feet the same, the lat er so much so that she
was obliged to have her suoes made larger.
“ I got little sleep at night,” she says, “ and was
in so much pain I hid to be propped up with pil-
lows. For weeks together 1 could not lie down in
bed. I had a dry, hollow cough, and bad night
sweats. Then diarrhoea set in and my bowels
became ulcerated I was often in dreadful agony
for forty-eight hours at a time. Then I would
have a chill as 'hough a bucket of cold water were
poured down my back. I got so low I could no
longer sew, knit, or do any housework or look
after my children. My sister had to come and
help in the house.
“ Everybody said I was in a decline and must
die. What I suffered for eight years tongue
cannot tell. The doctor could do nothing for me.
He said my complaint was complicated and bad to
deal with. In 1886 I wen tas an outdoor patient
to the Shrewsbury Infirmary, but only got
transient relief. ”
The writer is in good health now, but why did
her case remind me of the shipwreck ? Let’s settle
that first. The a-sociation is easy and natural.
Just see. The ship sank because we four men
hadn’t the strength to pump out the water as fast
as it came in. twenty men might have got her
into port. It is the last straw that breaks the
camel’s back ; the last unsupplied need that makes
poverty abject and desperate.
These bodies of ours carry the seeds of disease
with them all the time —chiefly the poisons created
by imperfect digestion, made worse by careless
habits. But as long as nothing extraordinary
happens we manage to scrape along in a half-and-
half sort of fashion. Yet We’ve got in our blood
the stuff that any of a dozen diseases is made of,
only waiting for something to set it afire. While
the liver, kidneys, and lungs and skin keep us
fairly free —that is, don’t let the load get too heavy
—we say, “ Oh, yes, I’m tolerably well, thank
you.” Little pains and unpleasant symptoms
bother us now and then, but we don’t fancy they
mean anything.
By-and-bye something happens. A cold, too
hearty a meal, a night of dissipation, an affliction
through death or loss of property, a fright, as in
Mrs. Bunco’s case, &c. Over we go. The last
straw has crushed us. One loose spark has blown
up the barrel of powder. The crew is too small to
save the ship. The kidneys, liver, skin, and
stomach strike work, and we must have help right
away or perish. All of which means the explosion
of latent indigestion and dyspepsia poisons in the
blood.
There 1 isn’t it plain why I thought of the ship ?
Now for the conclusion of the lady’s story. She
says : “In 1889 1 first heard of Mother Seigel’s
■Curative Syrup. Half a bottle made me feel
better, and by keeping on taking it I was soon
strong and well as ever. (Signed) Mrs. Ann
Bunce, The Park, Worthen, near Shrewsbury,
February 22nd, 1893.”
If there were only a way to save sinking ships
as certain and trustworthy as Mother Seigel’s
medicine is in the case of sinking human bodies,
•what a blessing it would be to poor sailors.*
The Story of a Signature .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the stirring times of native land-purchase in the
North Island of Mnorilund, when several wealthy
syndicates competed with each other for the in-
heritance of the Maoris, l)r. Buller, naturalist
and financial magnate, and Hon. Johnny Shee-
han, ex-Native and Defence Minister, reaped a
rich harvest of fees. This, in fact, was where
Buller made the fortune lie took to London to
spend, and which, I have no doubt, he is still en-
joying.
Sheehan made the more money of the two, but
he generally had it all spent some time before he
got it. Tiie two men were engaged by rival
syndicates, and they, in turn, engaged a staff of
agents, touts, interpreters and other border vaga-
bonds to do the routine work of the land-purchase
business. Both were expert Maori scholars, know-
ing not only the language, but every trait in the
' native character ; in addition to which they had a
powerful influence with the people generally.
The natives loved Sheehan as one of themselves,
for he sang their songs, danced their hakas, made
love to their wahines, slept in their whares, and
distributed his money and other favors with the
same lavishness as marked the bestowal of his
affections. He never refused to anyone anything
that he had it in his power to give. I have known
him receive <£soo in one sum in the morning
and not have a copper by sundown. His wine-
bill at the hotel where he stayed was seldom less
than .£lO a day ; at times it was very much more.
Indeed, I have known him spend as much as
£6O in one “ shout ” while perhaps a creditor was
waiting outside to dun him for a debt, most likely
incurred by some friend in his name. The veriest
stranger who could tell him a tale of woe about a
sick wife and a hungry baby could always reckon
on emptying his pockets, and if his pockets were
already empty he did not hesitate to pledge his
credit for what was wanted. When he went to
church on Sunday, the priest was generally richer
by .£5 or ,£lO when the service was over. I have
known him put a cheque on the plate for a fairly
large sum, and though it sometimes happened
that there was not sufficient at the bank to meet
it, still Johnny’s intention was good.
For a short session of the Lands Court he and
Buller would each receive, perhaps, <£soo as a
retainer and <£loo a day besides, not to speak of
occasional “refreshers ” and side practice. Buller
was known as the Pakira (or the Bald Head). He
was economical in his mode of living, and always
required discount on his bills. Yet he was at
times a man of large and wise hospitalities. Un-
like Sheehan, he had no creditors, and lived on
the sound principle of cash payments.
Sheehan’s great influence with the natives was
illustrated in a remarkable fashion when difficulty
was experienced in getting the whole of the native
owners in a block of land to sign away their
inheritance; for until every man, woman, and
child had affixed his, her, or its sign manual to
the deed of sale, no Crown grant could issue to
the purchasers. It often happened that one indi-
vidual declined to sell after the other members of
the hapu had affixed their signatures and obtained
their money, and to circumvent this obstruc-
tionist all kinds of devices were resorted to. A
bundle of notes were flashed before him or a bag
of gold jingled in his ear. If this did not bring
him to his senses a determined effort was made to
make him drunk and get at him in that way. This
latter device frequently succeeded when all else
had failed. Neither Sheehan nor Buller, I may
say, had anything to do with tins part of the busi-
ness.
The syndicate, which had taken in hand the
purchase of a block of some 500,000 acres in the
Auckland province, found itself in a disagreeable
corner for want of a signature. All the tribe had
signed with the exception of one old man
named Paraki, and until he put his pen to
paper the title could not be completed. Fully
.£lOO,OOO had already been sunk in securing the
property, but without Paraki’s signature the
money had been spent to no purpose. Every
known device was adopted to induce him to
sign. As much money was spent in making ihe
old man drunk as would have paid for his share
twice over. When they filled him up with rum he
would lie down where he stood and decline to talk
business until he became sober again. After this
they would give him presents, all of which failed
to bring him to a proper sense of duty. Two or
three times a day, perhaps, he would march into
the syndicate’s office where the parchment was
always ready, and, asking for a pen, would spread
himself out over the document and make a flourish
right over the spot reserved for his signature—
without, however, touching the paper. Then he
would look up at the anxious faces standing
around him, put down the pen with a smile, and
pulling his old blanket up round his naked loins,
would walk out of the office with a hurricane of
curses in his wake.
This went on for weeks, Paraki finally declining
to have anything whatever to do with, the deed,
despite the large sums of money offered him.
In this dilemma the syndicate appealed to
Sheehan.
“ We’ll give you .£lOO if you can get Paraki to
sign,” said the secretary.
“ You will give me £500,” said Sheehan.
The company declined the offer, but two days
after they told Sheehan that he could have the
£5OO.
“I want £lOOO now,” said Sheehan.
There was nothing else for it. Sheehan got his
thousand and the company got the signature.
Paraki, I think, got a new blanket.
J. F. Edgar.
Henry Lawson’s book, which may be
called “Block and Backblocks: Australian Verses
by Henry Lawson,” or something of that sort, will
be out shortly and will, like “ Banjo’s ” (orders
for which are rapidly flowing in), be a handsome
volume printed in new type on fine paper. The
price will be ss. 6d., post free from this office, and
The Bulletin, after seeing the material of which
the book is composed, has no hesitation in saying
that it deserves to be, and will be, an immediate
and permanent success.

What Killed Lowater
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When I heard about Lowater’s luck, I
thought I would go and congratulate that hero of
a hundred debts. No need to specify the part of
Sydney he lived in. He doesn’t live there,
now—no; anywhere else! He is dead, and I
fancy I’m about the only person who really knows
what killed him.
At top of a tall old house, overlooking one of the
least aesthetic corners of the harbor, dwelt
Lowater in a room for which there is every reason
to believe he had once paid a week’s rent. But
that had been two years ago, and as his landlady
—an ancient virgin with a hard eye and a hairy
chin —was, with all her other lodgers, a stern
exacter of her pound of flesh and a bit over, there
are grounds for the hypothesis that in her sour,
Calvinistic way she had come to accept the un-
remunerative Lowater as an unescapable develop-
ment of the essential cussedness of things. Here,
at all events, after weird vicissitudes of staircase,
if you were lucky, you didn't find Lowater ; if
you were unlucky, he was in, and promptly bor-
rowed half-a-crown of you.
But now all this was to be changed. F’ortuno
had “ let up ” on Lowater at last. An unexpected
death and a legacy in five figures had brought rest
for the sole of his financial foot.
Straddled across his solitary cha ; r, I found him,
looming vaguely through the cloud from the
burnt-out old briar that for years had warmed his
nose and assisted his cogitations. And, as with
the pipe, so with all! He hadn’t even treated
himself to the shave the undue postponement of
which he had been wont to declare was the pain-
fullest circumstance attaching to the non-posses-
sion of threepence sterling. The outer man, in
fact, was still as consistently and harmoniously-
shabby as the inner was obviously unelated ; and
altogether, as Lowater slouched over to the bed
from the chair he had vacated in my favor, he had
so little the air of a legatee in even two figures—
to say nothing of five ! —that I had my doubts, and
expressed them. I was wrong, though. The
thing was true enough, Lowater said. Close on
.£20,000. Uncle in Bristol West-Indian trade.
“Bopped off suddenly!”
“And opportunely!” I said. “Though, ’pon
my soul, old man ! anybody would think, from the
look of you, that it was an injury the old gentle-
man had done you !’ And then of course, I got
off a recondite and obvious witticism about the
change from Lowater to high- water.
A smile died young about the bristly corners of
his mouth, as he took from it his ancient pipe,
and stared as if, in it, he beheld symbolised
the order of things which was passing away.
“ I suppose I shall have to get a new one 1 ” I
heard him mutter, and then, as his glance dropped
to his feet—“ An i new boots ! D n new boots!
I hate ’em 1 As for clothes,’ he looked suddenly
up at me, “these togs are p -etty fair still, aren’t
they ? Bass muster anywhere, wouldn’t they I ”
“As a dress-suit for Guy F'awkes, Esquire,
they might! ” I said, and his fine fell further.
“I see how it is,” he said. “You think I
ought to be a bit more set up over this business
than I am ? ”
“Think? Why, man, look at the creditor-
haunted life you’ve had of it for years ! Hell-
upon-earth it would be for me, I know ! ”
“Yes,” he murmured, “yes! It has been a
bit rough. But,” taking up one knee and nursing
it reflectively, “I—you see—l’ve got accustomed
to it! ”
“You’ll find it a lot easier to get accus-
tomed to the other thing, my fine fellow!
Just think of all the schemings, and skulkings,
and borrowings, and—excuse me ! —lyings you’ve
been driven to ! You’re not going to tell me you
like that sort of thing, are you ? ”
Lowater dropped his leg and took up his pipe
again. “N—no I” he said, uneasily rubbing his
nose with the stem. “ I can hardly say that I
like it. But ” —I scarcely caught the muttered
words—“ I shall miss it, for all that —miss it most
damnably ! ”
# # *
Not till three months later did I meet
Lowater aga’n—coming out of a Pitt-stmet cafd,
well-dressed, well-lunched, and in jovial company
—but himself so far from jovial that I was fain to
ask if he’d been ill. He took leave of his friends
and walked a little way with me before he
answered. No ; he hadn’t been exactly ill ;
though, somehow, he didn’t feel altogether gay
either. I looked hard at his face. It was fleshier
than of old, and the peculiar, hunted expression
had faded; but plainly enough marked upon it
were the lines of a new worry and a worse.
“ Gambling, eh ? ”
“ No—very little in that way. Fact is, I’ve no
taste for it, any more than for liquor.”
“ Oh, well,” I said, “ in that case, we have only
to fall back upon the ”
“The woman?” he put in, quickly. “No,
you’re out of it again! Youd never guess,
unless—by the way, you don’t happen to have
half-a-crown about you, do you ? ”
I had ; and, wondering, I produced it.
“ Lend it me for a couple of weeks! ” said
Lowater, with so exactly the hurried half-shame of
his impecunious days that, wondering yet more, I
was handing over the coin when, with a mirthless
little laugh, he pushed back my hand.
“That’s the trouble!” he said. “Do you
remember my telling you that I should miss the
makeshifts and the dodges of the empty pocket ?
“Something of the kind. But, surely
“ That’s what s the matter, then 1 I d give any-
thing, this moment, to be badly in want of half-a-
crown, and to be compelled to borrow it! Good
day to you ! ” and he walked away.
“ Give anything ? ” I echoed as I stared after
him. “ Well, my friend, you have only to hand
over all your substance to those who don't thirst
after impecuniosity—one half-hours return to
which, unless Im vastly mistaken, would cure
you of about the strangest grievance any man ever
labored under! ”
• * •
“ Know a man called Lowater!”
“Lowater? —yes! That is, I did know him.
Haven’t seen him for nearly a year, though. Do
you know anybody of the name ?”
“Yes! —same man! I’ve heard him mention
your name. I’m attending him, you know—along
with Grubber.”
“ Attending him ? He’s ill, then ! What’s the
trouble ? ’
Dr. Skemlett looked me so hard in the face that
I saw he was sorely tempted towards an unpro-
fessional wink. “ Well, now, look here !” he
said. “ You’re sufficiently one of ourselves to
understand me when I say that we call it neuras-
thenia.”
I nodded. “For want of a better name. No
discoverable specific affection, eh ? ’
“ Thats it! The man, in fact, has no patholo-
gical business to die—but he’s dying, for all that!
Will you go and see him? Here’s his address.”
“ Thanks, I'll go. But, about dying; what does
Grubber say ?”
“ Oh, just what you’d expect him to say ! That,
where there’s no diagnosis, there can’t possibly be
any prognosis ! Rule-of-thumb man, old Grub-
ber ! Slay you, secundum artem, sooner than cure
you untraditionally. But this poor chap, Lowater,
is booked. If you don’t see him to-night or to-
morrow, you won’t see him alive—that’s all! ”
* • m
“ He is asleep ! ” whispered the nurse.
“No!” the sick man said, catching the slight
sound with a facility which was, in itself, a
desperately bad sign, “Who is it? Oh — you?
Well, I wanted to see you. Was it Dr. Skem-
lett ? ”
“ Yes ; he told me you were pretty bad.”
“Told you I was dying, you mean! Don’t
trouble to deny it. It doesn’t matter much. But
the point is—why am I dying? What am I dying
of? Don’t use long-tailed doctor’s words, now.”
Fused the shortest- “ tailed ” words I could
find. “ Don’t know,” I said.
“Well, I know; and I’ll tell you, because” —
his voice suddenly weakened—“you’ve had the
history of the case from the first.”
“I? Why, you were well enough last time I
saw you.”
A faint smile flickered over the death-pinched
features. “I didn’t look it,” he said—“as you
lost no time in telling me. But, go further back—
to the time before that. When I had just come in
for ”
His voice died away, and the nurse stooped
over him with a restorative. ‘‘ He must talk no
more,” she said.
“ For the legacy,” resumed the sick man, un-
heeding. “What did I tell you, then ? —as I told
you afterwards. About the old life, I mean I ”
“Well, you said you’d got accustomed to the
hard-up, hand-to-mouth way of existence —if
that's what you’re referring to ? ”
“Yes; and to the dun-dodging, and the bor-
rowing. and the scheming, and the glorious
uncertainty and dubiousness of it all! It was—a
game, and I his tones tapered feebly off again
—“ I missed, as I said I should, the excitement
of it—dam ’’
Again the nurse, with soft expostulation,
stooped over the face into which had stolen sud-
denly the last great greynoss. But into the dying
eyes came also something like a twinkle, as, with
a ghastly whimsicality of persistence, Lowater
forced out the latter half of the bisected word :
“ Nably ! ” he gasped ; then his eyelids fluttered
rapidly and fell.
“ Gone ? ' I said gently after a pause.
“Yes!” answered the nurse, with a cheerful
relaxation of manner that was like a blow.
“ Gone, sure enough ! A strange death, too ! I
never saw one like it! And I haven t the slight-
est idea, no w, what he died of. He said some-
thing about you understanding the case, didn’t
he? Do you know?’
The uniformed young person elevated a pair of
very pretty eyebrows, and I answered the un-
finished question.
“ Yes,’ I said; “I know what his complaint
wa>, It is a common one enough, though I never
knew it to end fatally before. He died of
crumenitis. ’
It was an illegitimate compound, forced upon
me by the fact that I couldn t, for the moment,
remember the Greek word tor purse ■, but it was
unquestionably inflammation of that important
organ, the treasury, that killed Lowater.
Alex. Montgomery.
“Temporary Insanity . ”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Ten o’clock ! Late—-but then I went tc
bed late—or early. Another day 1 1 often wonder
Why that infernal sun wants to rise every morn-
ing. . . . Another day I Well, I’m getting
very tired. It’s time the millennium came along.
* * #
“ Where’s that miserable book I’ve got to
translate, adapt, and condense into a short story ?
It’s a fearful thing to travel the world without
ideas and have to steal them from other people.
If a man is not rich he ought to be famous. When
he is neither he is merely a loafer, a beggar, a
thief, a hanger-on to the coat-tails of the world.
The world is mad for something new, and there
are only about six original ideas on earth. Six !
There’s not one—there’s nothing original—crea-
tion’s played out 1
# # #
“Good book, this. People I know in it.
Know them all. That Pesquinetis Harrison under
another name—l could swear to him anywhere :
lean-jawed, long, black-eyes, negro hair. And
this girl with the blue eyes, red lips. The very
woman 1 Little devil she was, too. And this
fellow that the whole gang are swindling and
working against. Me, for a certainty. A good
book, this. Damn good.
* * *
“ The hero does exactly what I would do,
and what I have done. He was just as tired of
everything as I am. He gets drunk on absinthe.
I’ve been drunk on almost every other liquor.
Well, his will be like another experience of my
own.
# « *
“He’s suffering a recovery! But more
tired than ever. Just like me. Why, this fellow
is my double. Clever chap, too —knows more
than I do. I’ll follow his lead.
« • #
“ Last chapter. Well, there’s only a few'
leaves left to finish it all up. It’s one of these
trick novels, perhaps—where there is a sudden
solution like the key-move of a chess problem.
I’m in an awful fix —I mean the hero is—at pre-
sent. Don’t see how I’m going to get out of it in
four pages either. . . , One. . . . Two.
. . . I’m getting deeper in the mire. What is
the solution ? , . , Three. . . . Four.
. . . Ha ! Cuts his way out! Outs his throat!
Yes—life is the Gordian Knot. Right. Where’s
my razor ?”
* « *

“ Not even its prevalence in royal families
has sufficed to make madness a fashionable
disease.” — Maudslky.
At Malvern Downs (Q.) shearing-shed two
shearers (Scotch and Australian) were disputing
as to their Biblical knowledge. Finally a tenner
was put up as to which of them could repeat the
“ Lord’s Prayer ” correctly. Sandy, after gauging
his man, starts, as a feeler, the 123rd Psalm—
“ The Lord is my shepherd, I shall not want,”
&o. The Cornstalk surlily interrupts —“ 0, take
the blessed stuff, you’ve won it; but I’ll fight yer
double or quits.”
